He declared it correct. And it is mentioned in al-Muntaka: If it fell on a rock and its belly split open, it is not eaten due to possibility of death from another cause. Al-Hakim al-Shahid said this, and he interpreted the absolute narration in al-Asl (al-Mabsut) as cases without split belly. Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi interpreted it as when the rock's edge struck it and split its belly thereby, and he interpreted the narration in al-Asl (al-Mabsut) as the animal not being struck by anything from the rock except what would strike it from the ground if it fell on it, and that is excused, and this is more correct. Translation: And in al-Muntaka it is mentioned that if the hunted animal fell on a rock and its belly split open, it will not be eaten due to the possibility of death from another cause, and al-Hakim al-Shahid declared this correct. He interpreted the absolute narration of al-Mabsut as applying to the case where the belly does not split. Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi interpreted al-Hakim al-Shahid's statement as referring to the animal that was struck by the rock's edge, so its belly split open due to that; and he interpreted the narration in al-Mabsut as the animal not being struck by anything from the rock except what it would be struck by from the ground had it fallen on it, and that is excused, and this is more correct. Explanation: Al-Muntaka is the book of al-Hakim al-Shahid; al-Mabsut is the work of Imam Muhammad; Imam al-Sarakhsi wrote a commentary on al-Mabsut which is very voluminous. Imam Muhammad said in al-Mabsut that if the hunted animal fell on a rock and settled, it is halal, whether its belly split open or not; his statement is absolute in this regard. Al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned in his book al-Muntaka that if the animal fell on a rock and its belly split open, it is haram and will not be eaten, because of the possibility of death from another cause besides the shot. He mentioned this and declared it correct. He also said that the ruling in al-Mabsut that it is halal applies only when the belly does not split; otherwise it is haram. Summary: Al-Hakim al-Shahid stated the issue as mentioned and interpreted the narration of al-Mabsut. Then the commentator of al-Mabsut, Imam al-Sarakhsi, considered both narrations from al-Muntaka and al-Mabsut and ruled that the application of al-Muntaka's narration is that if the animal fell on a rock and the rock's edge or point tore its belly, then it is haram. The application of al-Mabsut's narration is that if the animal fell on a rock and received a blow similar to what it would normally receive from falling on the ground, then that blow is excused. Therefore, if the animal fell on the ground and its belly split, it is excused and halal. Similarly, if it fell on a rock and its belly split due to a similar impact, it is also halal, because it received only the same amount of blow as from falling on the ground.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling when a hunted animal falls on a rock and its belly splits open. Imam Muhammad's absolute statement in al-Mabsut is that such an animal is halal, regardless of whether the belly splits. Al-Hakim al-Shahid, in his book al-Muntaka, reports that if the belly splits, it is haram due to the possibility of death from the rock's edge, and he interprets Imam Muhammad's statement as applying only when the belly does not split. Imam al-Sarakhsi, the great commentator of al-Mabsut, reconciles the two. He says: If the rock's edge or point causes a gash that splits the belly, then the death is attributed to that additional cause, so it is haram. But if the impact is like falling on the ground – meaning the belly splits due to the force of the fall itself, not a sharp edge – then it is halal. Sarakhsi's view is considered the most correct. This shows the Hanafi method of reconciling apparently conflicting narrations by analyzing the cause of death.

Key Terms
منتقی (al-Muntaka): A fiqh book by al-Hakim al-Shahid (d. 334 AH), containing selected rulings and narrations.
مبسوط (al-Mabsut): The foundational work of Hanafi fiqh by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, part of Zahir al-Riwayah.
دھار (dhar): The sharp edge of a rock or object that can cut or gash.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always, regardless of how the belly splits.
B) Never, because any split indicates additional cause.
C) Only when the rock's edge caused a gash.
D) When the split is due to blunt impact similar to falling on the ground.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on a hunted animal that falls on a rock and its belly splits depends on the cause: a gash from a sharp edge makes it haram, while a blunt impact like falling on ground makes it halal.

Ruling on Hunting with Pellets: Bunduq and the Condition of Cutting
Ruling statement with qiyas reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by a crushing projectile and explain the qiyas (analogy) to ma'rad.

Core Idea
Game killed by a pellet that crushes without cutting is haram because it resembles the ma'rad.

In this lesson, we examine a clear ruling from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar: game struck by a pellet (bunduq) that dies from the impact is not lawful to eat. The reason is that the pellet crushes and breaks the body but does not create a wound. This places it in the category of the ma'rad (blunt arrow). Understanding this distinction is essential for knowing which hunting implements render game halal.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
The text explicitly says 'do not eat' game killed by bunduq. Identify the act (consumption of game killed by pellet) and the hukm (haram).
The ruling is unconditional: no distinction is made for intention or other factors.
2
Identify the illa (cause)
The text provides the reason: because it crushes (tadu) and breaks (taksur) but does not wound (tajrah). The illa is that the projectile kills by crushing, not by cutting.
This is a jali (clear) illa based on observable effect.
3
Recognize the qiyas (analogy)
The text analogizes bunduq to ma'rad (blunt arrow), which is already known to produce non-halal game. The shared illa is killing by impact without cutting.
The analogy is explicit: 'fa sara ka al-mi'rad' (so it becomes like the ma'rad).
4
Extract the general principle
The underlying principle: for game to be halal when killed by a projectile, the implement must cut and cause a wound (jarh). If it only crushes, the animal is maytah (carrion).
This principle applies to all similar projectiles: shot, bullets, etc.
5
Apply to modern contexts
Consider modern firearms, air rifles, and shotguns. The ruling depends on whether the projectile cuts or crushes. For example, a bullet that penetrates and creates a wound channel may be considered cutting, while a solid pellet that flattens and crushes is not.
The key is the nature of the wound, not the speed or force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بندوق سے شکار کئے ہوئے جانور کا حکم
قال ولايوكل ما اصابه البندقة فمات بها لانها تدق وتكسر ولا تجرح فصار كالمعراض اذالم يخزق
ترجمه قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس کو غلہ لگاوہ اس سے مر گیا تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا اسلئے کہ غلہ کو تا ہے اور توڑتا ہے اور زخم نہیں کرتا تو وہ بے پھل کے تیر کے مثل ہو گیا جبکہ وہ اندر نہ گھسے
تشریح غلہ جو غلیل میں چلتا ہے اور ایسے ہی چھرہ اور ایسے ہی بندوق کی گولی کاملتی نہیں بلکہ پھاڑتی اور تو ڑتی ہے تو جب اس کی وجہ سے شکار مر گیا تو کھایا نہیں جائے گا اور یہ سب معراض کے درجہ میں ہیں
Book of Hunting: Ruling on game killed by a firearm/pellets. He (al-Quduri) said: And do not eat that which is hit by a pellet (bunduq) and dies from it, because it crushes and breaks and does not wound, so it becomes like a ma'rad (blunt arrow) when it does not pierce. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And that which is hit by a pellet and dies from it is not eaten, because the pellet crushes and breaks and does not cut, so it is like a blunt arrow when it does not enter. Explanation: Pellets that are shot from a slingshot, and similarly shot pellets and bullets, do not cut but rather tear and break. So when game dies from it, it is not eaten, and all these are in the category of ma'rad.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for game killed by a projectile that does not cut but crushes. The Arabic text from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar is quoted, followed by its Urdu translation and then a commentary. The ruling is that the game is not halal because the projectile (such as pellets from a slingshot, shot pellets, or bullet) does not create a wound (jarh) but instead crushes and breaks the body. It is analogized to the ma'rad, a blunt arrow without a head that kills by impact rather than cutting. Since the method of killing does not satisfy the requirement of causing a wound through a cutting implement, the animal is considered maytah (carrion) and is unlawful to consume. This ruling applies regardless of the specific type of projectile as long as it kills by crushing and tearing rather than cutting.

Key Terms
البندقة (al-bunduqah): A pellet or projectile shot from a slingshot or firearm, which kills by crushing.
المعراض (al-mi'rad): A blunt arrow without a head that kills by impact, not by cutting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of game depends on the projectile causing a wound (jarh) by cutting, not merely causing death.

Hunting with Stones: Condition of Weight and Edge
Explains four subcases of hunting with stones, deriving rulings through ta'lil (causation) and tafriq (differentiation).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each type of stone used in hunting and apply the condition of weight and edge to determine halal status.

Core Idea
For a stone-killed game, halal depends on weight and edge: light+cutting = halal; heavy or blunt = haram.

In previous lessons, we learned that game killed by a blunt arrow (ma'rad) is not halal because it kills by impact rather than slicing. The same principle applies to stones used for hunting. However, not all stones are alike: the Hanafi scholars (al-Quduri and later commentators) explain that the decisive factor is whether the stone's killing action is certainly due to cutting (jarh) or possibly due to crushing (thiqal). This lesson will walk through the four distinct sub-cases that derive from this single rule, showing how a subtle change in the stone's properties changes the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is hunting game by throwing a stone. The basic ruling is that if the stone kills by impact (like a ma'rad), it is not halal. If it kills by cutting, it may be halal. Your task is to identify the exact condition that flips the ruling.
The condition is not simply 'sharpness' but also weight.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The primary proof is the analogy (qiyas) to the ma'rad (blunt arrow). The ma'rad is known to kill by striking and crushing, not by cutting, so its game is haram. A stone that shares this characteristic—killing by weight—is also haram. Find how the Hanafi text uses this analogy.
The text states: 'it is like the ma'rad when it does not penetrate.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal reasoning)
The reasoning is based on the effective cause (illa): the stone kills with a possibility that death is caused by weight rather than the edge. For a heavy stone with an edge, there is doubt (ihtimal) that it killed by its heaviness. For a light stone with an edge, the doubt disappears because the weight is negligible, so death is certainly from the cut.
The doubt is the key: halal requires certainty that cutting caused death.
4
Apply the rule to the four sub-cases
The source lists four cases: (1) a plain stone (any weight, no edge?) kills by impact → not halal. (2) Heavy stone with edge → not halal due to doubt. (3) Light stone with edge → halal. (4) Light stone made long like an arrow with edge → halal. For each, state the ruling and the reason.
Cases 3 and 4 both satisfy the condition: light weight and cutting edge.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference between the Hanafi position and other schools (if any) is that the Hanafis require both lightness and edge for halal, while others might allow any sharp stone regardless of weight. Consider how this stricter condition affects hunters using slingshots or catapults.
Cross-madhhab: Shafi'is may allow if the stone cuts, regardless of weight.

Source Text

وکذلک ان رماه بحجر و کذلک ان جرحه قالوا تاويله اذا كان ثقيلا وبه حدة لاحتمال انه قتله بثقله وان كان الحجر خفيفاً وبه حدة يحل لتعين الموت بالجرح ولو كان الحجر خفيفا وجعله طويلا كالسهم وبه حدة فانه يحل لانه يقتله بجرحه

ترجمه قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس کو غلہ لگاوہ اس سے مر گیا تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا اسلئے کہ غلہ کو تا ہے اور توڑتا ہے اور زخم نہیں کرتا تو وہ بے پھل کے تیر کے مثل ہو گیا جبکہ وہ اندر نہ گھسے اور ایسے ہی (نہیں کھایا جائے گا ) اگر شکار کو پتھر پھینک کر مارا اور ایسے ہی ( نہیں کھایا جائیگا ) اگر پتھر نے اس کو زخمی کر دیا ہو فقہاء نے فرمایا ہے اس کی تاویل یہ ہے کہ جب کہ پتھر بھاری ہو اور اس میں دھار ہو اس بات کے احتمال کی وجہ سے کہ پتھر نے شکار کو اپنے بوجھ کی وجہ سے مارا ہو اور اگر پتھر ہلکا ہو حالانکہ اس میں دھار ہو تو حلال ہے موت کے متعین ہونے کی وجہ سے زخم لگانے کی وجہ سے اور اگر پتھر ہلکا ہو حالانکہ اس کو مثل تیر کے لمبا بنا رکھا ہے اور اس میں دھار ہے پس شکار حلال ہے اس لئے کہ یہ پتھر شکار کو اپنی دھار سے مار ڈالے گا۔

تشریح غلہ جو غلیل میں چلتا ہے اور ایسے ہی چھرہ اور ایسے ہی بندوق کی گولی کاملتی نہیں بلکہ پھاڑتی اور تو ڑتی ہے تو جب اس کی وجہ سے شکار مر گیا تو کھایا نہیں جائے گا اور یہ سب معراض کے درجہ میں ہیں پھر یہاں مصنف نے چند جزئیات پیش فرمائی ہیں۔ ا شکار کو پتھر مار کر مارڈالا تو وہ حلال نہیں ہوگا اسی دلیل مذکور کی وجہ سے۔ ۲۔ پتھر بھاری تھا اور اس میں دھار بھی ہے وہ شکار پر مارا اور شکار زخمی ہو گیا تو اب نہیں کھایا جائے گا کیونکہ اس میں ابھی یہ احتمال ہے کہ پتھر نے شکار کو اپنی دھار سے نہیں بلکہ بوجھ سے مارا ہے۔ ۳۔ پتھر ہلکا سا ہو اور اس میں دھار ہو اور اس کے مارنے کی وجہ سے شکار مر گیا ہو تو شکار حلال ہے کیونکہ یہاں یہ بات متعین ہے کہ زخم کی وجہ سے موت واقع ہوئی ہے۔ - پھر بلکا سا ہے جس کو تیر کے مثل لمبا اور طویل کر رکھا ہے اور اس میں دھار ہے تو اس کا شکار بھی حلال ہوگا اس لئے کہ یہ پتھر شکار کو زخم کرنے کی وجہ سے ماریگا نہ کہ اپنے بوجھ کی وجہ سے۔
And likewise if he throws a stone at it, and likewise if it wounds it, they said its interpretation is when the stone is heavy and has an edge, due to the possibility that it killed it by its weight. And if the stone is light and has an edge, it is halal because death is determined to be by the wound. And if the stone is light and he makes it long like an arrow and has an edge, then it is halal because it kills by its wound. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And that which is hit by a pellet... and likewise if he throws a stone at the game and it dies, it is not eaten; and likewise if the stone wounds it, it is not eaten. The scholars said its interpretation: when the stone is heavy and has an edge, due to the possibility that the stone killed it by its weight. And if the stone is light and has an edge, it is halal because death is determined to be by the wound. And if the stone is light and he makes it long like an arrow and has an edge, then the game is halal because this stone kills by its edge. Explanation: Pellets that are shot from a slingshot... Then the author presents some subcases: 1. If he kills game by throwing a stone, it is not halal due to the mentioned reason. 2. If a heavy stone with an edge wounds it, it is not eaten because there is a possibility that the stone killed it by its weight rather than its edge. 3. If a light stone with an edge kills it, the game is halal because death is certain to be from the wound. 4. If a light stone is made long like an arrow and has an edge, its game is halal because it kills by wounding, not by weight.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling for game killed by a stone. The matn states the basic principle: if a stone is thrown and kills by crushing, it is not halal; but if the stone cuts and death is by the wound, it is halal. The ta'wil (interpretation) given by the scholars explains that a heavy stone with an edge raises doubt that death might be due to the weight rather than the cut, so it is not halal. A light stone with an edge removes that doubt because the stone's weight is insignificant, so death is ascribed to the cut. Similarly, if a light stone is shaped like an arrow with an edge, it clearly cuts, so the game is halal. The Urdu commentary then lists four specific cases derived from this principle: (1) a plain stone that kills by impact is not halal; (2) a heavy stone with edge that wounds is still not halal due to doubt; (3) a light stone with edge that wounds is halal; (4) a light stone elongated like an arrow with edge is halal. In all cases, the determining factor is whether the death is confidently attributed to the cutting action of the stone as opposed to its weight.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajar): a stone used as a projectile in hunting

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for game killed by a light stone with an edge, according to the Hanafi school?
A) Haram because it is a stone
B) Halal because death is certainly due to the wound
C) Haram because of doubt
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
The halal status of game killed by a stone hinges on whether death is certainly from the cut (light edge stone) or possibly from the weight (heavy stone, even with an edge).

Ruling on Game Killed by a Sharp Marble Stone (Mawrat)
Explains that game killed by mawrat is haram even if it cuts due to doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by a mawrat stone and explain the role of doubt (shakk) in the prohibition.

Core Idea
Sharp or not, game killed by mawrat is haram because doubt about the cause of death makes it like carrion.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition addresses a specific hunting tool: a sharp marble stone called mawrat. The ruling establishes that even when such a stone cuts the jugular veins, the game is not halal. The key principle is doubt – if the animal may have died from the impact rather than the cut, the meat is treated as carrion (maytah). Understanding this helps you apply the broader Hanafi rule that certainty is required for halal slaughter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the instrument
Note that mawrat is a sharp, stone-like tool, hard enough to cut. Recognize that the passage discusses two scenarios: when it does not cut and when it does cut.
The sharpness alone does not guarantee halal.
2
Analyze the first scenario (no cut)
If the stone strikes and kills without cutting, the death is by crushing (dagan), which is not permissible. The meat is haram.
This is clear-cut.
3
Analyze the second scenario (cutting)
Even if the stone cuts the head or jugular veins, the ruling remains haram. The reason is doubt: the veins could have been severed by the weight of the stone, and the animal might have died before the cut completed.
This is the unique point of the passage.
4
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The argument is based on shakk (doubt). Since we cannot be certain that death was caused solely by the cutting, the animal is considered maytah. In Hanafi law, certainty is required for halal slaughter.
Compare with proper dhabh where the cut is deliberate and controlled.
5
Apply the ruling
The practical outcome: any game killed by a thrown mawrat is haram, regardless of whether it cuts. This ruling safeguards the principle that halal meat requires a proper slaughter that ensures death by cutting, not by impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رماه بمروة حديدة ولم تبضع بضعا لا يحل لانه قتله دقا كذا اذا رماه بها فا بان راسه او قطع اوداجه لان العروق تنقطع بثقل الحجر كما تنقطع بالقطع فوقع الشك او لعله مات قبل قطع الأوداج

ترجمہ اور اگر اس کو دھار دار سنگ مروہ کے ساتھ مارا اور اس نے کسی عضو کو نہیں کاٹا تو وہ حلال نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ اس نے شکار کو تو ڑ کر مارا ہے اور ایسے ہی ( حلال نہیں ہے ) جبکہ شکار کو سنگ مروہ سے مارا ہو پس اس کا سر جدا کر دیا یا اس کی رگیں ( رگ گردن و حلقوم و مری ) کاٹ دیں اس لئے کہ رگیں کٹ جاتی ہیں پتھر کے بوجھ سے جیسا کہ کٹ جاتی ہیں کاٹنے سے تو شک پیدا ہو گیا کہ شاید شکار مر گیا ہو رگوں کے کٹنے سے پہلے ہی۔

تشریح مروہ ایک سخت قسم کا پتھر ہے جس کی صفوان بھی کہا جاتا ہے جو چھری کی طرح تیز ہوتا ہے جس سے جانور ذبح کر دیا جاتا ہے اس کا واحد مروہ ہے یہ بھی جزئیات ہیں چار پہلے گزر چکی ہیں۔ ۵- سنگ مروہ جو دھاردار ہے وہ شکار پر پھینکا اور اس نے شکار کا بدن نہیں کاٹا اور وہ شکار مر گیا تو یوں سمجھا جائیگا کہ پتھر نے شکار کو کوٹ کر اور پھوڑ کر مارا ہے نہ کہ کاٹ کر لہذا یہ حلال نہ ہو گا۔
- یہی پتھر پھینکا جس سے شکار کا سر الگ ہو گیا یا اس کی رگیں کٹ گئیں اب بھی شکار حلال نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس میں شک پیدا ہو گیا، اور وجہ شک یہ ہے کہ رگیں جس طرح کاٹنے سے کٹ جاتی ہیں اور جدا ہو جاتی ہیں اسی طرح پتھر کی چوٹ سے بھی الگ ہو جاتی ہیں نیز یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ پتھر کی چوٹ کی وجہ سے شکار رگیں کٹنے سے پہلے ہی مر چکا ہو بہر حال شک کی وجہ سے اس کو کھانا حلال نہ ہوگا۔
And if he throws a sharp mawrat stone at it and it does not cut, it is not halal because it killed it by crushing. Likewise, if he throws it and it separates its head or cuts its jugular veins, it is not halal because the veins are severed by the weight of the stone just as they are severed by cutting, so doubt arises, or perhaps it died before the veins were cut. Translation: And if he strikes it with a sharp mawrat stone and it does not cut a limb, it will not be halal because it killed it by crushing. And likewise, it is not halal when the stone cuts off its head or severs its veins, because the veins are severed by the weight of the stone just as they are severed by cutting, so doubt arises that perhaps the animal died before the veins were cut. Explanation: Marwah is a hard type of stone, also called safwan, which is as sharp as a knife and used for slaughtering. Its singular is mawrat. These are also subcases; four have passed. 5. If a sharp mawrat stone is thrown at game and it does not cut the body and the game dies, it will be understood that the stone killed by crushing and breaking, not by cutting, so it is not halal. 6. If the same stone is thrown and it separates the head or cuts the veins, even then the game is not halal because doubt arises. The reason for doubt is that veins can be separated by the impact of the stone just as they are separated by cutting, and also it is possible that the animal died from the impact before the veins were cut. In any case, due to doubt, it is not halal.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage deals with a sharp marble stone (mawrat, also called safwan). The ruling: if such a stone is thrown and does not cut the body, it is not halal because it kills by crushing. If it cuts off the head or severs the jugular veins, it is still not halal because of doubt: the veins could have been severed by the impact of the stone (by its weight) rather than by cutting, and the animal might have died before the veins were cut. Thus, due to the doubt about the cause of death, the game is considered unlawful. The Urdu commentary adds that mawrat is a hard, very sharp stone like a knife used for slaughtering. However, if it is thrown and does not cut, it is like a blunt instrument. Even if it cuts, the doubt remains, so it is haram.

Key Terms
مروة (mawrah): A hard, sharp stone used for slaughtering, also called safwan.
شك (shakk): Doubt about the cause of death, which triggers prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the stone is too hard
B) Because there is doubt that the animal might have died from the impact before being cut
C) Because mawrat is not allowed for hunting
D) Because the stone is too light

Key Takeaway
Game killed by a mawrat stone is haram because doubt about the cause of death makes it equivalent to carrion.

Ruling on Hunting with a Stick or Staff
States that game killed by a stick is haram unless it has a cutting edge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by a stick and identify the condition that makes it permissible.

Core Idea
Game killed by blunt force is haram; only a cutting edge makes it halal.

This ruling addresses a common scenario: hunting with a stick or staff. The Hanafi school distinguishes between tools that kill by weight (blunt force) and those that kill by cutting. The default is prohibition, but an exception exists for tools that have a sharp edge and actually cut the game, placing them in the same category as a sword or spear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is throwing a stick or staff at game, causing death. The default ruling is that the meat is not halal (haram).
The key legal cause (illa) is that it kills by weight, not by wound.
2
Understand the legal cause
The stick kills by blunt force (thaqal), not by cutting (jarh). This is the basis for the prohibition.
Tools that kill by weight do not legally count as proper hunting instruments.
3
Identify the exception
If the stick has a sharp edge (hiddah) that cuts (yabda'u), then the game becomes halal.
The edge must actually cut the body, not just be sharp.
4
Reason for the exception
Such a stick is considered analogous (bi manzilati) to a sword or spear, which are legitimate hunting tools.
This analogy (qiyas) is based on the common effective cause (illa) of cutting.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, one must determine whether the tool cuts or kills by blunt force. Only if it cuts is the meat halal.
This is a condition (shart) for permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رماه بعصا او بعود حتى قتله لا يحل لانه يقتله ثقلا لا جرحا اللهم الا اذا كان له حدة يبضع بضعا فحينئذ لا بأس به لانه بمنزلة السيف والرمح

ترجمہ اور اگر شکار کو لاٹھی یا چھڑی ماری ہو یہاں تک کہ اس کو مارڈالا تو وہ حلال نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ یہ ( لاٹھی اور چھڑی) شکار کو بوجھ سے مارتی ہے نہ کہ زخم کر کے مگر جبکہ اس کے دھار ہو کہ وہ بدن کاٹ دے تو اس وقت اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ وہ تلوار اور نیزے کے درجہ میں ہے۔

تشریح یہ بھی ایک جزئیہ ہے،
(۷) اگر شکار پر لاٹھی یا چھٹڑی پھینکی جس نے وہ مر گیا تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے کیونکہ لاٹھی اور چھڑی کاٹتی نہیں ہے بلکہ اپنے بوجھ سے پھوڑتی ہیں ہاں اگر لاٹھی اور چھڑی میں ایسی دھار ہو جو بدن کو چیر سکتی ہے اور کاٹ سکتی ہے تو اب ان کو تلوار اور نیزے کے درجہ میں رکھا
And if he throws a stick or piece of wood at it until it kills it, it is not halal because it kills by weight, not by wound. Unless it has an edge that cuts, then there is no harm because it is in the category of a sword and spear. Translation: And if he strikes the game with a stick or staff until it kills it, it will not be halal because these (stick and staff) kill by weight, not by wound. But if it has an edge that cuts the body, then there is no harm because it is in the category of a sword and spear. Explanation: This is also a subcase. (7) If a stick or staff is thrown at game and it dies, it is not halal because sticks and staffs do not cut but break by their weight. However, if the stick or staff has such an edge that can cut and sever, then it is considered in the category of a sword and spear.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This ruling covers game killed by a stick or staff. Since sticks are blunt and kill by blunt force, the game is not halal. However, if the stick has a sharp edge that cuts the body, it is permissible because it then functions like a sword or spear, which are legitimate hunting tools. The Urdu commentary lists this as the seventh case in the series of sub-rulings.

Key Terms
عصا (asa): a stick, staff, or club
عود (ud): a piece of wood, staff

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When the stick has a sharp edge that cuts
C) When the stick is thrown hard
D) When the stick is made of wood

Key Takeaway
Game killed by a stick is halal only if the stick has a cutting edge; otherwise it is haram.

Hunting with a Sharp Stone: When Crushing Invalidates Halal Status
Ruling on game killed by a sharp stone that does not cut

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by a sharp stone that does not cut, and identify the condition of certainty for permissible consumption.

Core Idea
Game killed by a sharp stone's weight, not its cut, is not halal — the cause of death must be a wound, not crushing.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses the use of a sharp stone (marwah) in hunting. The Hanafi school requires that the animal's death be conclusively caused by a cut (jarh), not by the stone's weight or impact. Even if the stone cuts off the head or severs veins, the meat is not halal if there is doubt whether the animal died before the cut was complete. Let's analyze the two scenarios presented.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is hunting game using a sharp stone (marwah). The passage rules on the permissibility of consuming the meat.
The tool is considered sharp (hadid) but that alone is insufficient.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
First scenario: the stone does not cut the animal, kills by crushing. Second scenario: the stone cuts off the head or severs the jugular veins.
Both result in the same ruling: not halal.
3
Understand the legal cause (illah)
In the first case, death is from crushing (daqq), not a wound. In the second, doubt (shakk) arises because the veins may have been severed by the stone's weight rather than by a clean cut, and the animal may have died before the cut.
Certainty is required; doubt leads to prohibition (ihtiyat).
4
Apply the ruling
The game is not halal in either scenario. The Hanafi position is that the cause of death must be a wound (jarh) produced by a sharp tool.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Source Text

احات البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴
۱۵۷
كتاب الصيد
سنگ مرمر جو تیز دھار والا ہے شکار پر پھینکا اور اس نے شکار کا بدن نہیں کاٹا اور وہ شکار مر گیا تو وہ شکار کھایا جائے گا یا نہیں
ولو رماه بمروة حديدة ولم تبضع بضعا لا يحل لانه قتله دقاو كذا اذا رماه بها فا بان راسه او قطع اوداجه لان العروق تنقطع بثقل الحجر كما تنقطع بالقطع فوقع الشك او لعله مات قبل قطع الأوداج ترجمہ اور اگر اس کو دھار دار سنگ مروہ کے ساتھ مارا اور اس نے کسی عضو کو نہیں کاٹا تو وہ حلال نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ اس نے شکار کو تو ڑ کر مارا ہے اور ایسے ہی ( حلال نہیں ہے ) جبکہ شکار کو سنگ مروہ سے مارا ہو پس اس کا سر جدا کر دیا یا اس کی رگیں ( رگ گردن و حلقوم و مری ) کاٹ دیں اس لئے کہ رگیں کٹ جاتی ہیں پتھر کے بوجھ سے جیسا کہ کٹ جاتی ہیں کاٹنے سے تو شک پیدا ہو گیا کہ شاید شکار مر گیا ہو رگوں کے کٹنے سے پہلے ہی۔ تشریح مروہ ایک سخت قسم کا پتھر ہے جس کی صفوان بھی کہا جاتا ہے جو چھری کی طرح تیز ہوتا ہے جس سے جانور ذبح کر دیا جاتا ہے اس کا واحد مروہ ہے یہ بھی جزئیات ہیں چار پہلے گزر چکی ہیں۔ ۵- سنگ مروہ جو دھاردار ہے وہ شکار پر پھینکا اور اس نے شکار کا بدن نہیں کاٹا اور وہ شکار مر گیا تو یوں سمجھا جائیگا کہ پتھر نے شکار کو کوٹ کر اور پھوڑ کر مارا ہے نہ کہ کاٹ کر لہذا یہ حلال نہ ہو گا۔
- یہی پتھر پھینکا جس سے شکار کا سر الگ ہو گیا یا اس کی رگیں کٹ گئیں اب بھی شکار حلال نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس میں شک پیدا ہو گیا، اور وجہ شک یہ ہے کہ رگیں جس طرح کاٹنے سے کٹ جاتی ہیں اور جدا ہو جاتی ہیں اسی طرح پتھر کی چوٹ سے بھی الگ ہو جاتی ہیں نیز یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ پتھر کی چوٹ کی وجہ سے شکار رگیں کٹنے سے پہلے ہی مر چکا ہو بہر حال شک کی وجہ سے اس کو کھانا حلال نہ ہوگا۔
And if he threw at it a sharp stone and it did not cut a part, it is not permissible because he killed it by crushing. Similarly, if he threw at it and its head separated or its jugular veins were cut, because the veins are severed by the weight of the stone as they are severed by cutting, so doubt occurs that perhaps it died before the cutting of the veins.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on hunting with a sharp stone called marwah. If the stone kills by crushing without cutting, the game is not halal because the method of killing is by weight (thuql) not by wound (jarh). Even if the stone cuts off the head or severs the jugular veins, the game remains not halal due to doubt: the veins might have been severed by the stone's impact weight, not by a clean cut, and the animal may have died before the veins were fully cut. Hence caution (ihtiyat) dictates prohibition.

Key Terms
مروة (marwah): A hard, sharp stone used for slaughtering, also called safwan.
بضع (bada'a): To cut or lacerate the body, a wound that separates tissue.
دق (daqqa): Crushing or pounding; death by impact/weight rather than cutting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter throws a sharp stone (marwah) at game and the stone does not cut the animal but kills it by crushing, what is the ruling?
A) Halal because the stone is sharp.
B) Halal because the stone can cut.
C) Not halal because death is from crushing, not cutting.
D) Not halal because the stone is not iron.

Key Takeaway
Game killed by a sharp stone that does not cut or where the cause of death is uncertain is not halal; the Hanafi school requires certainty that death is from a wound.

Hunting with a Stick or Wood: The Condition of Cutting
Ruling on hunting with a stick or wood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by a stick or wood and identify the condition that makes it halal.

Core Idea
A stick kills by weight, not wound — so the game is haram unless the stick cuts.

In hunting, the means of killing matters. A sharp-edged tool like a sword or spear cuts and makes the game halal. But what about a blunt stick? The Hanafi school addresses this with a clear distinction between killing by weight (thuql) and by wound (jarh). This lesson examines the ruling and the exceptional case.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
Identify the default ruling for game killed by a stick or wood: it is not halal.
The text says 'la yahillu' (it is not permissible).
2
Understand the cause
Recognize that the stick kills by weight (thuql), not by wound (jarh).
Weight means the force of impact, not a cut.
3
Identify the exceptional condition
Determine the condition under which the game becomes halal: the stick must have sharpness (hiddah) that cuts.
The word 'allahumma' emphasizes the rarity of such a stick.
4
Grasp the analogy
Understand that such a sharp stick is treated like a sword or spear (bi manzilat al-sayf wa al-rumh).
Sword and spear are classic tools that wound and make game halal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار پر لاٹھی یا چھٹڑی پھینکی اور وہ مر گیا تو حرام ہے
ولو رماه بعصا او بعود حتى قتله لا يحل لانه يقتله ثقلا لا جرحا اللهم الا اذا كان له حدة يبضع بضعا فحينئذ لا بأس به لانه بمنزلة السيف والرمح
ترجمہ اور اگر شکار کو لاٹھی یا چھڑی ماری ہو یہاں تک کہ اس کو مارڈالا تو وہ حلال نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ یہ ( لاٹھی اور چھڑی) شکار کو بوجھ سے مارتی ہے نہ کہ زخم کر کے مگر جبکہ اس کے دھار ہو کہ وہ بدن کاٹ دے تو اس وقت اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ وہ تلوار اور نیزے کے درجہ میں ہے۔ تشریح یہ بھی ایک جزئیہ ہے،
(۷) اگر شکار پر لاٹھی یا چھٹڑی پھینکی جس نے وہ مر گیا تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے کیونکہ لاٹھی اور چھڑی کاٹتی نہیں ہے بلکہ اپنے بوجھ سے پھوڑتی ہیں ہاں اگر لاٹھی اور چھڑی میں ایسی دھار ہو جو بدن کو چیر سکتی ہے اور کاٹ سکتی ہے تو اب ان کو تلوار اور نیزے کے درجہ میں رکھا جائے گا اور جانور حلال ہوگا۔ تنبيه اللھم ۔ اس کلمہ کو استثناء سے پہلے اس وقت استعمال کر لیا جاتا ہے جبکہ مستثنی عزیز و نادر ہو جس سے اس کی قدرت و شذوذ کی جانب اشارہ ہوتا ہے۔
And if he threw at it a stick or a piece of wood until he killed it, it is not permissible because he kills it by weight, not by wound. Except if it has a sharpness that cuts, then there is no harm because it is in the level of a sword or spear.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh subsidiary issue (jaz'iyah). A stick or wood normally kills by the force of impact (thuql), not by cutting (jarh). Therefore, the game is not halal. However, if the stick or wood has a sharp edge that can cut and wound, then it is treated like a sword or spear, and the game is halal. The word 'allahumma' is used before the exception to indicate that the exception is rare and unusual, emphasizing its rarity.

Key Terms
عصا (asa): A stick, staff, or rod used for striking.
حدة (hiddah): Sharpness; the quality of being able to cut.
اللهم (allahumma): An exclamation used before an exception to indicate rarity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter throws a wooden stick at a bird, killing it. The stick has no sharp edge and does not cut. What is the ruling?
A) Halal because wood is a natural material.
B) Halal because the stick caused death.
C) Not halal because the stick kills by weight.
D) Not halal because wood is impure.

Key Takeaway
A stick or wood kills by weight, not by wound; only when it has sharpness does the game become halal.

General Principle: Death by Wound vs. Weight
General principle determining halal/haram based on cause of death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi general principle for determining the halal status of game when the cause of death is uncertain, and apply the precautionary ruling in cases of doubt.

Core Idea
Certainty of cause decides halal/haram; doubt triggers haram as precaution.

In hunting, the precise cause of death often determines whether the game is permissible or prohibited. This lesson presents the foundational Hanafi principle that resolves such cases: if death is certainly from a wound, the animal is halal; if certainly from weight (blunt force), it is haram; and if uncertain, it is haram as a precaution. This rule anchors the rulings of numerous specific scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two certain attributions
The principle distinguishes two clear causes of death: wound (جرح) and weight/impact (ثقل). Understand that each yields a definitive ruling when known with certainty.
2
Apply the ruling for certainty
If death is certainly attributed to a wound, the game is halal. If certainly to weight, it is haram. Memorize this binary outcome.
Certainty here means no doubt about the causal link.
3
Handle doubt with caution
When the cause is unknown — could be wound or weight — the ruling is haram as a precaution (ihtiyatan). This is a key Hanafi approach to protect against consuming haram.
This applies even if the object used was normally cutting.
4
Connect to the key term 'mudaf'
The term 'mudaf' (مضاف) indicates the attribution of death. The ruling hinges on to which cause death is 'added' with certainty.
Attribution is a legal causation concept.
5
Apply the principle
Given a scenario, determine if death is certain from wound, certain from weight, or doubtful, then state the corresponding ruling.
Practice with examples from the previous cases in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدہ کلیہ
والاصل في هذه المسائل ان الموت اذا كان مضافا الى الجرح بيقين كان الصيد حلا لا واذا كان مضافا الــى الـثـقــل بـيـقـيـن كـان حـرامـا وان وقـع الشك ولا يدرى مات بالجرح او بالثقل كان حراما احتياطاً ترجمہ اور ان مسائل میں قاعدہ کلیہ یہ ہے کہ موت جبکہ یقین کے ساتھ زخم کی جانب مضاف ہو تو شکار حلال ہوگا اور جبکہ یقین کے ساتھ بوجھ کی جانب مضاف ہو تو حرام ہو گا اور اگر شک ہو جائے اور یہ معلوم نہ ہو سکے کہ زخم کی وجہ سے مرا ہے یا بوجھ سے مرا ہو تو حرام اور اگر یہ معلوم نہ ہو سکا ہو تو بھی احتیاطی حرام ہوگا۔
The general principle in these issues: if death is attributed with certainty to a wound, then the game is halal; and if it is attributed with certainty to weight, then it is haram; and if doubt occurs and it is not known whether it died from the wound or from the weight, then it is haram as a precaution.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is a foundational principle (qa'idah kulliyyah) that summarizes the rulings of all previous cases. The Hanafi school maintains a careful approach: certainty of the cause of death determines halal status. When doubt exists, the stricter ruling (haram) is applied out of caution (ihtiyat). This principle is derived from the general rule that blood and meat are prohibited unless the animal is properly slaughtered or hunted with a wound that causes blood flow.

Key Terms
مضاف (mudaf): Attributed or ascribed; the death is causally linked to either wound or weight.
احتياطاً (ihtiyatan): As a precaution; applying the stricter ruling to avoid potential prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general principle, if a hunter strikes game with a sharp object, but there is doubt whether it died from the wound or from the impact, what is the ruling?
A) Halal because the object was sharp.
B) Halal because there is no certainty of haram.
C) Haram because of doubt.
D) Permissible due to necessity.

Key Takeaway
Certainty of attribution to wound or weight determines halal/haram; when uncertain, the game is haram as a precaution.

Hunting with a Sword or Knife: The Condition of Wounding
Ruling on killing game with a sword or knife based on which part strikes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting with a sword or knife and identify the condition that makes the game halal or haram.

Core Idea
For hunting to be halal, the weapon must cut and wound — crushing by weight does not make game permissible.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in hunting: when a hunter throws a sword or knife at game. The critical factor is not the material of the weapon but which part strikes the animal. The Hanafi school distinguishes between killing by wounding (halal) and killing by crushing (not halal). Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the laws of hunting (sayd) correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two outcomes
The act being considered is throwing a sword or knife at game. The outcome depends on which part strikes: if the sharp edge (hadd) wounds the animal, it is halal; if the back of the knife (qafa) or the hilt (maqbid) strikes, it is not halal.
The act is explicitly throwing (ramā), not striking with the hand.
2
Locate the key condition (shart)
The condition for halal is that the weapon must inflict a wound (jarh) and cut (qat'). If death occurs without cutting – for example by crushing (daws) – the game is not halal.
The text says: 'إذا اصابه بحده فجرحه حل' (if it strikes with its edge and wounds it, it is halal).
3
Understand the effective cause (illah)
The underlying reason is that halal hunting requires death by wounding, not by weight. A sword or knife kills by weight when the blunt part hits, which is equivalent to killing by throwing a heavy object.
The commentary explains: 'الحديد وغيره فيه سواء' – iron and other materials are equal; the focus is on the action of cutting.
4
Apply the ruling to similar cases
If any weapon – regardless of material – kills by cutting, it is halal. If it kills by blunt force, it is not. This principle extends to arrows, spears, etc.
For example, a sharpened wooden stick that cuts would be halal; a heavy metal bar that crushes would not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار کی طرف تلوار یا چھری پھینکی اور وہ شکار مر گیا تو حلال ہے یا حرام
وان رماه بسیف او بسکین فاصابه بحده فجرحه حل وان اصابه بقفا السكين او بمقبض السيف لا يحل لإنه قتله دقا والحديد وغيره فيه سواء
ترجمہ اور اگر شکار پر تلوار یا چھڑی پھینکی ہو پس وہ شکار کو گی اپنی دھار کی طرف سے پس اس کو زخمی کر دیا تو حلال ہے اور اگر اس کو لگی ہو چھری کی پشت کی طرف سے یا تلوار کا قبضہ تو حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ ان سے (ان دونوں میں ہر ایک نے اس وقت شکار کوٹ کنارا ہے اور لوہا اور غیر لوہا اس میں برابر ہے۔
تشریح یہ آٹھواں جزئیہ ہے شکار کو تلوار یا چھری پھینک کر ماری تو اگر دھار کی طرف سے لگ گئی اور جانور زخمی ہو کر مر گیا تب تو حلال ہے اور اگر تلوار کی پشت شکار کونگی یا تلوار کا قبضہ لگا تو حلال نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس وقت شکار ان کے بوجھ سے مرا ہے اور یہ کاٹنا نہیں بلکہ تو رو کوٹ ہے والحديد وغيره فيه سواء اور لوہا اور غیر لوہا اس میں برابر ہیں، یعنی شکار میں جرح وقطع شرط ہے خواہ لو ہا ہو یا اور کچھ ہو لہذا اگر لوہے کی دھار دار چیز سے مارا مگر دھار سے زخم نہیں بلکہ چوٹ سے مر گیا تو حرام ہے جیسے یہاں مذکور ہے تو خلاصہ کلام مدارلو ہے پر نہیں بلکہ مقصد پر ہے اور وہ جرح وقطع ہے
And if he threw at it a sword or a knife, and it struck it with its sharp edge and wounded it, then it is halal. And if it struck it with the back of the knife or the hilt of the sword, it is not halal because he killed it by crushing. And iron and other materials are equal in this.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is the eighth subsidiary issue. The ruling depends on which part of the weapon strikes the game. If the sharp edge (hadd) inflicts a wound (jarh), the game is halal because death is from cutting. If the blunt part (back of the knife or hilt of the sword) strikes, death is from crushing (daws), so it is haram. The material (iron or other) does not matter; only the action of cutting is considered. The focus is on the presence of a wound (jarh/qat').

Key Terms
قفا السكين (qafa al-sikkin): The back of the knife (opposite the blade).
مقبض السيف (maqbid al-sayf): The hilt or handle of the sword.
جرح وقطع (jarh wa qat'): Wounding and cutting; the essential condition for halal hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter throws a knife at a deer. The knife hits the deer with its back (non-blade side), killing it. What is the ruling?
A) Halal because it was thrown with an intent to hunt.
B) Halal because a knife is a sharp tool.
C) Not halal because it killed by weight.
D) Not halal because deer are not to be hunted with knives.

Key Takeaway
Halal hunting requires death by wounding (jarh/qat'), not by crushing or weight, regardless of the weapon's material.

Hunting with an Arrow: Is Bleeding Required?
Ruling on arrow wounds with and without blood flow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the condition of bleeding in arrow hunting and evaluate the disagreement among later scholars.

Core Idea
Bleeding is not strictly required if the wound is present; blood may be trapped.

When hunting with an arrow, a clear wound that causes bleeding is the ideal case and is unanimously halal. But what if the arrow wounds the animal and it dies, yet no blood is visible? Some later Hanafi scholars argue that bleeding is still a condition based on the hadith, while others consider a wound sufficient even without visible blood, citing the possibility of blood being trapped. This lesson unpacks both views and the evidence behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the unanimous case
The passage states that if the wound is bleeding (mudmiyan), the game is halal by agreement (bil ittifaq). Recognize this as the baseline ruling.
2
Locate the point of disagreement
The dispute arises when the wound is not bleeding. The text introduces two later opinions: 1) it is still halal, and 2) bleeding is required.
3
Examine the first opinion and its reasoning
Some later scholars say it is halal regardless of visible blood, because blood may be trapped due to a narrow wound opening or thick blood. The wound itself causes death.
This view relies on the effectiveness of the wound, not the external appearance.
4
Examine the second opinion and its proof
Other scholars require bleeding, citing the hadith: 'ما أنهر الدم وأفرى الأوداج فكل' (Whatever causes blood to flow and severs the jugular veins, eat). They interpret 'أنهر الدم' as a condition for halal.
The hadith is a dalil naqli, but its interpretation is zanni.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: if an animal dies from an arrow wound without visible blood, some declare it halal, others haram. The text does not give tarjih, but later Hanafi fatwa tends to accept the first view.
This shows how a difference in understanding a dalil leads to different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار کو تیر مارا اس سے وہ زخمی ہو گیا اور پھر مر گیا تو حلال ہے یا حرام
ولو رماه فجرحه ومات بالجرح ان كان الجرح مدميا يحل بالاتفاق وان لم يكن مدميا فكذلك عند بعض المتاخرين سواء كانت الجراحة صغيرة أو كبيرة لان الدم قد يحتبس بضيق المنفذ او غلظ الدم وعند بعضهم يشترط الادماء لقوله عليه السلام ما انهر الدم وافرى الاوادج فكل شرط الانهار
ترجمہ اور اگر شکار کو تیر مارا پس اس کو زخمی کر دیا اور شکار زخم کی وجہ سے مرگیا اگر زخم خون آلود ہو تو بالا تفاق حلال ہے اور اگر خون آلود نہ ہو
And if he shot it with an arrow and wounded it and it died from the wound: if the wound is bleeding, it is halal by agreement; and if it is not bleeding, then according to some later scholars it is also halal, whether the wound is small or large, because blood may be trapped due to narrow passage or thickness of blood; and according to some, bleeding is a condition because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whatever causes blood to flow and severs the jugular veins, eat.'
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage addresses a point of difference among later scholars (mutakhkhirin) regarding arrow wounds. If the arrow creates a wound that bleeds, the game is halal by consensus (bil ittifaq). If the wound does not bleed, some later scholars still consider it halal because blood might be trapped inside the body due to narrow wound opening or thick blood. Others require bleeding, citing the hadith: 'Whatever causes blood to flow and severs the jugular veins, eat.' The Hanafi view is that bleeding is not strictly required as long as there is a wound that would normally cause blood to flow, but the presence of blood is evidence of a valid wound. The text presents both views without giving tarjih.

Key Terms
مدميا (mudmiyan): Bleeding; a wound that produces blood.
الادماء (al-idma'): Causing blood to flow; the state of making blood appear.
أفرى الأوداج (afra al-awdaj): Splitting or cutting the jugular veins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a hunter shoots an arrow that wounds the game and the game dies, but no blood is visible from the wound, what is the ruling?
A) Haram by consensus.
B) Halal by consensus.
C) There is disagreement among later scholars.
D) Halal only if the arrowhead is iron.

Key Takeaway
When hunting with an arrow, a bleeding wound is unanimously halal; when no bleeding occurs, there is a legitimate difference among later Hanafi scholars, with the preponderant view being that the wound itself suffices.

The Bleeding Condition in Hunting – Later Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Explaining the condition of blood flow for hunted game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition of bleeding (idma') to different hunting scenarios and identify when the game becomes permissible according to the later Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
Hunted game is halal only when blood flows from the wound, though some later Hanafis allow large wounds to suffice if bleeding does not appear.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we encounter a nuanced ruling on the requirement of blood flow in hunted game. The text presents three situations: the size of the wound, the case of slaughter without bleeding, and hitting a non-lethal part like hoof or horn. The later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirin) differed on the first two, while the third is a clear condition. This lesson will help you navigate these scenarios and understand the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Scenarios
Read the passage and note the three distinct cases discussed: (1) large wound vs small wound, (2) slaughter of a sheep without blood flow, (3) arrow hitting hoof or horn. Each has a ruling or ikhtilaf.
2
Analyze the Ikhtilaf on Large Wound
The phrase "when some of them" indicates a disagreement among later Hanafis. Understand the basis: if the wound is large, some say it's halal without bleeding; others require bleeding regardless. Ask yourself: what is the illa (effective cause) for halalness?
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Understand the Two Opinions on Slaughter without Blood
The passage says "it is said... and it is said" – two opinions. Connect this to the previous discussion: the reasoning for both is included in what was mentioned. Consider how the large wound exception might apply here.
4
Apply the General Rule to the Hoof/Horn Case
The text gives a clear ruling: if the arrow hits the hoof or horn and causes bleeding, it's halal; otherwise not. This is a straightforward application of the idma' condition and supports the stricter view.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi Position on Idma'
Bring the three scenarios together. The general principle requires idma', but later scholars introduced an exception for large wounds. The hoof/horn case reinforces the general rule. Recognize that the passage does not settle on one mufta bihi.
The authority_weight indicates this is from Zahir al-Riwayah but the ikhtilaf is among later scholars.

وعند بعضهم ان كانت كبيرة حل بدون الادماء وانكانت صغيرة لا بد من الادماء ولو ذبح شاة ولم يسل منه الدم قيل لا تحل وقيل تحل ووجه القولين دخل فيما ذكرنا واذا اصاب السهم ظلف الصيد او قرنه فان ادماه حل والافلا وهذا يؤيد بعض ما ذكرناه

And when the arrow hits the hoof or horn of the game, if it makes it bleed, it is permissible, otherwise not. And this supports some of what we mentioned.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a ruling about the requirement of blood flow (idma') in hunted game. The text begins by stating that according to some late scholars (muta'akhkhirin), if the wound is large, the game is halal even without visible bleeding, because blood may stop due to the narrowness of the wound or thickness of blood. If the wound is small, then bleeding is necessary. Then the text extends this to the case of slaughtering a sheep: if blood does not flow, there are two opinions (one saying it is not halal, another saying it is halal). The reasoning for both is tied to the previous discussion. Finally, if an arrow hits the hoof or horn of the game: if it causes bleeding, the game is halal; otherwise it is not. This supports the view that bleeding is a condition for halalness, as stated by some scholars like Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar. The passage does not settle on one ruling but presents the ikhtilaf among later Hanafis.

Key Terms
الإدماء (al-idma'): The act of causing blood to flow from a wound; in hunting, the condition that blood must be shed for the game to be halal.
ظلف (zulf): The hoof of an animal; specifically of game animals like deer.
قرن (qarn): The horn of an animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

In Hanafi fiqh, the flow of blood (idma’) is a key condition for the halalness of hunted game, though a minority among later scholars excuse it for large wounds.

Ruling on the Cut Limb of Hunted Game
Explaining the ruling when a hunted animal's limb is cut off by an arrow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating a limb cut from hunted game and explain the proof from hadith, contrasting it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A limb cut from a living hunted animal is haram; the animal itself is halal.

Hunting with arrows raises questions about what parts may be eaten. While the game animal is halal, what about a limb severed by the arrow? The Hanafi school draws a distinction based on a prophetic hadith, leading to a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. This passage explores the proof and reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the Hanafi position
Understand that the Hanafis rule the cut limb is haram, but the game animal is halal. Note the explicit statement in the matn: 'the game is eaten, and the limb is not eaten.'
The limb is not permissible to eat regardless of whether the animal dies from the wound.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that both the animal and the cut limb are halal if the animal dies from the wound. His reasoning: the cut limb is removed by 'slaughter of necessity', analogous to voluntary slaughter where the head is cut off.
If the animal does not die from the wound, the Shafi'i also considers the limb haram.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil naqli)
The Hanafi proof is the hadith: 'Whatever is cut off from a living animal is dead.' The limb is cut while the animal is alive (life remains in the body), so the limb is dead meat (maytah) and haram.
The hadith is absolute and applies to any living animal.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i proof (qiyas)
The Shafi'i argue by analogy to voluntary slaughter: if the head is cut off, both head and body are halal. Similarly, a limb cut by hunting should be halal if the animal dies from the wound, as both are acts of slaughter.
The Hanafi response distinguishes: in voluntary slaughter, the animal dies by the act, but in hunting, the limb is cut while life remains.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: Hanafis do not eat the cut limb; Shafi'is do if the animal dies. The Hanafi view is mufta bihi due to the clear hadith and the weakness of the analogy.
The ruling applies to any part (limb, ear, tail) cut while the animal is alive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا رمى صيدا فقطع عضوا منه اكل الصيد لما بيناه ولا يوكل العضو وقال الشافعى اكل ان مات الصيد منه لانه مبان بذكاة الاضطرار فيحل المبان والمبان منه كما اذا ابين الرأس بذكاة الاختيار بخلاف ما اذا لم يمت لانه ما ابين بالذكاة ولنا قوله عليه السلام ما ابين من الحى فهو ميت ذكر الحى مطلقا فينصرف الى الحي حقيقة وحكما العضو المبان بهذه الصفة لان المبان منه حى حقيقة لقيام الحيوة فيه وكذا حكما لانه تتوهم سلامته بعد هذه الجراحة ولهذا اعتبره الشرع حتى لو وقع في الماء وفيه بهذه الصفة يحرم
He (al-Quduri) said: And if he shoots a game and cuts off a limb from it, the game is eaten, based on what we explained, and the limb is not eaten. And al-Shafi'i said: Both are eaten if the game dies from it, because it is separated by the slaughter of necessity (dhakat al-istirar), so the separated part and that from which it is separated become permissible, just as when the head is separated by optional slaughter (dhakat al-ikhtiyar). Contrary to when it does not die, because it is not separated by slaughter. And our proof is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whatever is cut off from a living animal is dead.' He mentioned 'living' absolutely, so it refers to living in reality and in legal ruling. The cut limb has this description because that from which it is cut is living in reality due to the existence of life in it, and also legally because one imagines its safety after this wound. Therefore, the law considers it such that if it falls into water while having this description, it is prohibited.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the ruling of a limb cut off from a hunted animal. The matn from al-Quduri states that the game animal itself is halal (because it is killed by a permissible means, i.e., a shot that causes a wound), but the cut-off limb is not eaten. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that if the game dies from the wound, both the animal and the cut limb are halal, because the cutting of the limb is considered a form of 'dhakat al-istirar' (slaughter of necessity) analogous to when the head is cut off in voluntary slaughter. However, if the animal does not die from the wound, then the limb is not halal. The Hanafis respond with a proof: the hadith 'Whatever is cut off from a living animal is dead.' The Prophet (s) said 'living' in an absolute sense, meaning truly alive and also legally considered alive. The limb cut off from a living animal is considered dead and therefore not permissible to eat. The Hanafi argument emphasizes that the animal from which the limb is cut is still alive (both actually and legally), so the cut piece is treated as dead meat (maytah). This reasoning is so strong that if the animal later falls into water while still alive with such a wound, it is haram (because death by drowning is not causing blood to flow). The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi.

Key Terms
مبان (muban): A limb or part that is separated from the body; in this context, a limb cut off by an arrow during hunting.
ذكاة الاضطرار (dhakat al-istirar): Slaughter of necessity, i.e., killing by hunting with an arrow or bullet, as opposed to voluntary slaughter with a knife.
ذكاة الاختيار (dhakat al-ikhtiyar): Optional or voluntary slaughter of a domestic animal by cutting the throat in the prescribed manner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on eating a limb cut off from a hunted animal that dies from the wound?
A) Both the animal and the limb are halal.
B) The animal is halal, but the limb is not.
C) The limb is halal only if it is the head.
D) Neither the animal nor the limb is halal.

Key Takeaway
A limb cut from a living hunted animal is haram, even if the animal dies from the wound, because the hadith declares such a part dead meat (maytah).

Ruling on a Limb Cut from a Living Animal (Muban min al-Hayy)
Explains ruling, Shafi'i dispute, and general principle for limb cut from live animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on muban min al-hayy, evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument, and apply the general principle to determine when such a limb is halal or haram.

Core Idea
A limb cut from an animal with real and legal life is haram; if only pictorial life remains, it is halal.

In the Hanafi school, the ruling on a limb cut from a living animal (muban min al-hayy) is not absolute; it hinges on whether the animal possesses both real life (presence of spirit) and legal life (ability to survive after separation). This lesson examines the Hidayah's treatment of this issue, including the proof from hadith, the response to Imam al-Shafi'i's opposing view, and the derivation of a general principle that governs multiple scenarios.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: a limb cut from an animal that has both real life (spirit present) and legal life (can survive independently) is haram. Understand that this is based on the hadith 'What is cut from a living animal is dead.'
The term 'living' here refers to the complete individual with both types of life.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the limb is separated by a necessary dhakat (dhakat al-istirar), so it should be halal like properly slaughtered meat.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
At the moment of cutting, the animal is still alive, so no dhakat has occurred. After death, the limb lacks life and cannot benefit from the dhakat of the principal (tabi') because followership is severed by separation.
The response refutes both the timing and the followership argument.
4
Determine the general principle
The derived principle: what is separated from an animal that is truly and legally alive is haram; what is separated from an animal that is only pictorially alive (like a slaughtered animal with residual movement) is halal.
This principle is the key takeaway from the dispute.
5
Apply the principle to specific cases
Work through the branched rulings: cutting a hand, leg, thigh, or less than half the head makes the cut part haram but the remainder halal. Splitting into halves or cutting from the buttocks makes both parts halal because life cannot be imagined in the remainder.
These cases illustrate how the principle operates in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴
كتاب الصيد
کہ اس میں حقیقتا اور حکما حیوۃ ہو، جس شکار سے عضو الگ ہوا ہے تو بوقت علیحدگی اس میں حقیقی حیات موجود ہے یہ حقیقی حیات ہے اور اس عضو کے الگ ہونے کے بعد اگر یہ زندہ رہ سکتا ہو تو یہ اس کی حکمی حیات ہے جب یہ تقریر ذہن نشین ہوگئی تو اب دلیل سمجھئے حدیث میں آیا ہے کہ ما ابين من الحى فهو ميت جو عضوحی کے بدن سے جدا کیا گیا ہو اور کاٹا گیا ہو وہ میت ہے اور میت حرام ہے۔ تو حضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے یہاں مطلق می (زندہ) ارشاد فرمایا ہے تو اس سے فرد کامل مراد ہو گا اور فرد کامل وہ ہے جو ہم نے ذکر کیا ہے کہ شکار میں فی الحال حیات ہے اور آئندہ زندہ رہ سکتا ہے اول حقیقی اور ثانی حکمی ہے۔ تو جب زندہ کے بدن کا ٹکڑا حرام ہے تو یہ عضو بھی حرام ہوگا کیونکہ مبان منہ ابھی زندہ ہے اور آئندہ اس کی زندگی مشتمل ہے اور یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر اس کی محتمل ہو اور وہ ایسی حالت میں پانی میں گر جائے تو وہ حرام ہوگا اور یوں کہا جائے گا کہ پانی نے اس کو ہلاک کر دیا ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ شریعت نے ایسی حیات کا اعتبار کیا اور ابھی اسکوجی شمار کیا ہے اور جی کا عضو بنص حدیث حرام ہے۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل اور اس کا جواب
وقوله ابين بالذكاة قلنا وقوعه لم تقع ذكاة لبقاء الروح فى الباقى وعند زواله لا تظهر في المبان بعدم الحيوة فيه ولا تبعية لزوالها بالانفصال فصار هذا الحرف هو الاصل ان المبان من الحي حقيقة وحكما لا يحل والمبان من الحي صورة لا حكما يحل
ترجمہ اور شافعی کا قول کہ اس کو ذکاۃ سے جدا کیا گیا ہے تو ہم جواب دیں گے کہ اس کے وقوع کے وقت وہ ذکاۃ واقع نہیں ہوئی باقی شکار میں روح کے باقی رہنے کی وجہ سے اور روح کے زوال کے وقت ذکاۃ مبان میں ظاہر نہ ہوگی اس میں حیات نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے اور تابع کرنا نہیں ہوسکتا تبعیت کے زائل ہو جانے کی وجہ سے جدا ہونے کی وجہ سے پس یہی نکتہ قاعدہ کلیہ ٹھہرا کہ جو ٹکڑا جدا کیا گیا ہو ایسے جی سے جو حقیقتا اور حکما حی ہو تو حلال نہ ہوگا اور جو جدا کیا گیا ہو ایسے جی سے جو صورۃ کی ہو نہ کہ حکما تو وہ حلال ہے۔ تشریح امام شافعی نے اپنے استدلال میں فرمایا تھا لانه مبان بذكاة الاضطرار کہ یہ عضوذ کا ۃ اضطراری کی وجہ سے جدا ہوا ہے تو حلال ہونا چاہیے۔ ان کے اسی استدلال کو مخفف کر کے کہا ہے کہ ان کا قول ابين بالذکاۃ۔
بہر حال یہ امام شافعی کا استدلال تھا یہ ذکاۃ کا اثر ہے تو حضرت مصنف نے جواب دیا کہ حضرت امام شافعی صاحب ! جب ابھی شکار میں روح باقی ہے اور وہ زندہ ہے تو آپ اس کو ابھی ذکاۃ کیسے کہہ سکتے ہیں کیونکہ جب یہ عضو جدا ہوا ہے تو شکار زندہ ہے اور اس میں روح باقی ہے۔
سوال امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ ابھی تو زندہ ہے لیکن جب وہ شکار مرے گا تو ذکات متحقق ہو گئی ؟ جواب ... جب وہ عضو الگ ہو گیا اور اس میں نہ حیات ہے نہ روح تو شکار کے روح نکلنے کی وجہ سے ذکاۃ شکار میں پائی جائے گی کہ عضو میں کیونکہ ذکاۃ کی حقیقت زخم سے روح کا نکلنا ہے اور یہاں تو اس عضو میں سرے سے روح ہے ہی نہیں سوال . امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ ہاں بات آپ کی صحیح ہے مگر شکار متبوع ہے اور عضو اس کے تابع ہے اور متبوع کا حکم تابع کے لئے ثابت ہوا کرتا ہے لہذ ا متبوع کی ذکاۃ تابع یعنی عضو کی بھی زکاۃ ہوگی؟ جواب حضرت متبوع اور تابع کی بات تو جب درست ہوتی جبکہ عضو جدا نہ ہوتا اور جب وہ جدا ہو گیا تو تبیعت ہی زائل ہوگئی۔ پس اس تقریر سے ایک جنرل پوائنٹ نکالا اور وہی اس باب کا قاعدہ کلیہ ہے کہ جو عضو ایسے شکار سے الگ ہو جو ہر یہ اور حکما زندہ ہو تو وہ حرام ہو گا (كما مر) اور اگر ایسے شکار سے جدا ہو جو حقیقیہ زندہ ہو اور حکما زندہ نہ ہو جبکہ مردہ ہو تو وہ حلال ہو گا اب ضرورت ہے کہ اس کی مثال سامنے آئے تو مصنف اس کی مثال پیش فرماتے ہیں۔
مذکورہ مسئلہ کی نظیر
وذلك بان يبقى في المبان منه حيوة بقدر ما يكون فى المذبوح فانه حيوة صورة لا حكما ولهذا لو وقع في الماء وبه هذا القدر من الحيوة او تردى من جبل اوسطح لا يحرم فتخرج عليه المسائل
ترجمہ اور یہ اس طریقہ پر کہ مبان منہ میں اتنی حیات باقی رہے جتنی مذبوح میں رہتی ہے تو یہ صورۃ حیوۃ ہے نہ کہ حکما اور اسی وجہ سے اگر وہ پانی میں گر جائے اور اس میں اس قدر حیات ہو یا پہاڑ یا چھت سے لڑھک جائے تو وہ حرام نہ ہوگا پس اس قانون کلی پر مسائل کی تخریج ہوئی ہے تشریح یہ سابق میں بیان فرمودہ جنرل پوائنٹ کی مثال ہے کہ وہ شکار جس کا عضو کٹا ہے اگر اس میں اتنی حیات ہو جتنی مذبوح میں ہوتی ہے تو یہ فقط صورة حیات ہے حکما نہیں ہے لہذا اگر وہ پانی میں گر جائے تو حرام نہ ہوگا کیونکہ مرنا پانی کی وجہ سے نہیں بلکہ زخم کی وجہ سے ہے اسی طرح اگر وہ اس حال میں پہاڑ وغیرہ میں گرے اور لڑھک جائے تو بھی حرام نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ اس کا مرنا زخم کی وجہ سے ہے نہ کہ لڑھکنے کی وجہ سے اب مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ ایسا قاعدہ کلیہ ہے جس پر بہت سارے مسائل متفرع ہوتے ہیں اب کچھ جزئیات متفرع بیان کی جائیں گی۔
قاعدہ مذکورہ پر متفرع ہونے والی جزئیات
فنقول اذا قطع يدا اور جلا او فخذا اوثلثه مما يلى القوائم او اقل من نصف الرأس يحرم المبان ويحل المبان منه لانه يتوهم بقاء الحيوة فى الباقى ولو قده بنصفين او قطعة اثلثا والاكثر مما يلى العجز او قطع نصف رأسه او اكثر منه يحل المبان و المان منه لان الميان منه حى صورة لا حكما اذ لا يتوهم بقاء الحيوة بعد هذا الجرح
جبکہ کاٹ دیا ہاتھ یا پیر باران یا شکار کا ثث جو ہاتھ پاؤں سے متصل ہے یا آدھے سر سے کم تو مبان حرام ہے اور مبان منہ
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu Sharh Hidayah Volume 14. Chapter of Hunting: That in it there is real and legal life. The game from which the limb is separated: at the time of separation, real life exists in it; this is real life. And after the limb separates, if it can survive, this is its legal life. When this explanation is understood, now understand the proof: In the hadith it came: 'What is cut from a living animal is dead' – a limb separated from the body of a living animal is dead, and the dead is forbidden. So the Prophet (peace be upon him) said absolutely 'living', so from this is meant the complete individual, which we mentioned: that the game currently has life and can survive in the future; the first is real and the second is legal. So when a piece of a living body is forbidden, this limb is also forbidden because what is separated from it is still living and its life continues. And this is the reason that if it is possible and it falls into water in such a state, it will be forbidden, and it will be said that water killed it. So it is known that the Shari'ah considers such life and counts it as life, and the limb of a living animal is forbidden by the text of the hadith.
Imam Shafi'i's proof and its answer: And his saying: 'It is separated by dhakat (ritual slaughter).' We say: At its occurrence, dhakat did not occur because of the remaining spirit in the rest, and at the time of its (the spirit's) departure, dhakat does not become apparent in the separated part due to lack of life in it; nor is it a follower (tabi') because of the cessation of followership by separation. So this point became the general principle: What is separated from a living animal, truly and legally, is not permissible; and what is separated from an animal that is only pictorially (sura) living, not legally, is permissible.
Translation: And Shafi'i's saying that it is separated by dhakat, we will answer that at the time of its occurrence that dhakat did not occur due to the spirit remaining in the rest of the game; and at the time of the spirit's departure, dhakat will not appear in the separated part because there is no life in it; and followership is not possible because followership is removed by separation. So this point became the general principle: A piece separated from an animal that is truly and legally alive will not be permissible; and a piece separated from an animal that is only pictorially alive, not legally, is permissible. Explanation: Imam Shafi'i in his argument said: 'Because it is separated by a necessary dhakat' – that this limb is separated by a necessary dhakat, so it should be permissible. This summary of his argument is that his saying is 'separated by dhakat.' In any case, this was Imam Shafi'i's argument that this is the effect of dhakat. So the author (al-Marghinani) replied: O Imam Shafi'i! When the spirit still remains in the game and it is alive, how can you call this a dhakat? Because when this limb separated, the game was alive and the spirit remained in it.
Question: Imam Shafi'i says: It is still alive, but when that game dies, dhakat becomes actualized? Answer: When the limb became separated and there is no life or spirit in it, then due to the spirit leaving the game, dhakat will be found in the game, because the reality of dhakat is the exit of spirit from the wound, and here there is no spirit at all in this limb. Question: Imam Shafi'i says: Yes, your statement is correct, but the game is the principal (matbu') and the limb is its follower (tabi'), and the ruling of the principal is established for the follower; therefore, the dhakat of the principal will also be the dhakat for the follower, i.e., the limb? Answer: The matter of principal and follower is correct only when the limb is not separated; when it becomes separated, followership itself is nullified. So from this discussion, a general point is extracted, and that is the general principle of this chapter: A limb that separates from game that is truly and legally alive will be forbidden; and if it separates from game that is truly alive but not legally (i.e., it is practically dead), then it will be permissible. Now examples are needed, so the author presents examples.
Analogy of the mentioned issue: 'And that is such that in the separated part remains life to the extent that remains in a slaughtered animal, for it is life pictorially, not legally; and therefore if it falls into water while having this amount of life, or falls from a mountain or a roof, it is not forbidden; and upon this are built the rulings.'
Translation: And this is in the manner that in the part from which it was separated, so much life remains as remains in a slaughtered animal, then this is pictorial life, not legal. And for this reason, if it falls into water while it has this amount of life, or falls from a mountain or a roof, it will not be forbidden. So upon this general principle, rulings are derived. Explanation: This is an example of the general point mentioned earlier: if the game from which the limb is cut has as much life as ordinarily remains in a slaughtered animal, then it is only pictorial life, not legal. Therefore, if it falls into water, it will not be forbidden because its death is due to the wound, not water. Similarly, if it falls from a mountain etc. and rolls, it will not be forbidden because its death is due to the wound, not the fall. Now the author says that this is a general principle upon which many rulings are branched. Now some branched details will be mentioned.
Branched rulings upon the mentioned principle: 'So we say: If he cuts a hand, a leg, a thigh, or a third of it from the side of the limbs, or less than half of the head, the cut part is forbidden and the part from which it was cut (the remainder) is permissible, because it is imagined that life remains in the rest. If he splits it into two halves, or cuts it into thirds, or the majority from the side of the buttocks, or cuts half or more of its head, the cut part and the part from which it was cut are both permissible, because the part from which it was cut is alive pictorially, not legally, since life is not imagined to remain after this wound.'
When he cuts a hand, a leg, a thigh, or a third that is attached to the limbs, or less than half of the head, the cut part is forbidden and the part from which it was cut is permissible.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the ruling on a limb cut from a living animal (muban min al-hayy) in the context of hunting. It begins by establishing the Hanafi position: a limb cut from an animal that is both truly and legally alive (i.e., it can survive independently) is haram based on the hadith 'What is cut from a living animal is dead,' meaning it is treated as carrion. The author explains that 'living' here refers to the complete individual with both real and legal life. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the limb is separated by a necessary dhakat, so it should be halal. The Hanafi response is twofold: First, at the moment of cutting, the animal is still alive, so no dhakat has occurred. Second, when the spirit departs, dhakat does not apply to the separated limb because it lacks life, and the principle of followership (tabi') fails because the limb is already separated. The general principle derived is: a limb separated from an animal that is truly and legally alive is haram; from one that is only pictorially alive (like a slaughtered animal with residual movement) is halal. Examples are given: if the remainder has life comparable to that of a slaughtered animal, it is only pictorial life; falling into water or from a height does not render it haram because death is attributed to the wound. Finally, the author lists specific cases: cutting a limb near the legs or less than half the head makes the cut part haram but the rest permissible; cutting into halves or from the buttocks makes both parts permissible because life cannot be imagined in the remainder. This passage exemplifies the systematic Hanafi method of extracting legal rulings from primary texts and responding to opposing views.

Key Terms
مبان (muban): A limb or part cut off from an animal.
ذکاة (dhakat): Ritual slaughter that makes meat halal.
حیوہ حقیقیہ و حکمیہ (hayat haqiqiyyah wa hukmiyyah): Real life (presence of spirit) and legal life (ability to survive after separation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a limb cut from a hunted animal considered haram?
A) Always, because any part cut from a living animal is carrion.
B) Only if the animal has both real and legal life at the time of cutting.
C) Only if the animal is a domestic animal.
D) Never, because it is considered dhakat.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on muban min al-hayy hinges on the distinction between real and legal life; the limb is only haram if both exist.
