intermediate
8 min
The Ruling on a Limb Cut from a Hunted Animal
Establishes a general rule for cut limbs from hunted animals with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi general rule to determine when a cut limb from a hunted animal is halal or haram based on the animal's expectation of survival.

Core Idea
A cut limb is haram if the animal could have survived; otherwise both parts are halal.

The hunted animal is lawful through the wound inflicted by the hunter. But what if a limb is cut off while the animal is still alive? Is that limb lawful or not? Imam al-Shafi‘i argued that the limb is subordinate to the animal, so its slaughter is accomplished by the animal's. The Hanafi school responds that subordination ceases with separation. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi general rule (qaidah kulliyyah) that distinguishes between life in form (hayat surah) and life in legal effect (hayat hukman).

Guided Path
1
Understand the legal question and the Shafi‘i objection
The core issue: a limb is cut from a live hunted animal. Imam al-Shafi‘i says the limb is halal because the animal’s slaughter covers its parts (subordination). Identify this argument before examining the Hanafi response.
The subordination argument (taba'iyyah) is a common principle in fiqh.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response: separation ends subordination
The Hanafi school argues that subordination only applies while the limb is attached. Once separated, the limb is an independent piece of meat that was not properly slaughtered. Hence it is haram unless the animal was already legally dead at the moment of separation.
This is the key point of ikhtilaf between the schools.
3
Learn the general rule (qaidah kulliyyah)
The passage states: if the animal retains life equivalent to that of a normally slaughtered animal (madhbuh), the cut limb is haram; if the animal has only formal life (i.e., inevitable death), both the limb and the animal are halal.
Formal life means the animal moves but is fatally wounded.
4
Apply the rule to examples given in the text
Work through the two categories: (a) cuts where survival is expected (e.g., cutting a hand, foot, thigh, less than half the head) → limb haram, rest halal. (b) cuts where survival is impossible (e.g., cutting in half, severing most of the head) → both halal.
The criterion is 'tawahhum' – reasonable expectation of survival.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference with Imam al-Shafi‘i is that he would rule the limb halal in all cases because of subordination. The Hanafi ruling is more restrictive: it prohibits the limb when the animal could have survived.
This affects what parts of the hunted animal may be eaten.

كتاب الصيد
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴ حلال ہے اس لئے کہ باقی میں حیواۃ کے باقی رہنے کا تو ہم ہے اور اگر اس کے دوٹکڑے کر دیئے یا تین ٹکڑے کر دیئے اور جو حصہ نیچے کئے دھڑ سے متصل ہے وہ اگر کاٹ دیا یا اس کا آدھا سر یا اس میں اکثر کاٹ دیا تو مبان اور مبان منہ دونوں حلال ہیں اس لئے کہ مبان منہ صورۂ زندہ ہے نہ کہ حکما اس لئے کہ اس زخم کے بعد حیات کے باقی رہنے کا تو ھم نہیں ہے۔ تشریح یہ قاعدہ مذکورہ پر تفرع ہونے والی جزئیات میں پہلی تمام امثلہ میں شکار کے زندہ رہنے کا تو ھم تھا اسلئے شکار کو حلال اور عضو کو حرام قرار دیا اور دوسری تمام امثلہ میں اس کو تو ہم نہیں ہے کہ وہ زندہ رہ سکے تو یہاں دونوں کو حلال قرارد یا باقی تفصیل ترجمہ سے ظاہر ہے۔
Book of Hunting. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary on Hidayah Volume 14. Because the reality of slaughter (dhakat) is the exit of the soul from the wound, and here in that limb there is no soul at all. Question: Imam al-Shafi'i says: Yes, your statement is correct, but the hunted animal is the principal (matbu') and the limb is its subordinate (tabi'), and the ruling of the principal is established for the subordinate. So the slaughter of the principal becomes slaughter of the subordinate as well? Answer: The discussion of principal and subordinate is valid when the limb is not separate; when it becomes separate, subordination ceases. From this discussion a general point is extracted, which is the general rule of this chapter: a limb that separates from a hunted animal that is actually and legally alive is haram; and if it separates from an animal that is actually alive but not legally alive (i.e., dead), it is halal. Now an example is needed, and the author presents it. The analogy of the mentioned issue: That is, if there remains in the cut-off part (muban minhu) life equivalent to that which remains in a normally slaughtered animal (madhbuh), then it is life in form (surah) not in legal effect (hukman). Therefore, if it falls into water with this amount of life, or falls from a mountain or roof, it is not forbidden. So issues are derived from this general rule. Explanation: This is an example of the previously mentioned general point: if the hunted animal whose limb is cut has life equal to that of a slaughtered animal, it is only formal life, not legal. Hence, if it falls into water, it is not forbidden because death is due to the wound, not water. Similarly, if it falls from a mountain etc., it is not forbidden. The author says this is a general rule on which many issues are branched. Now some branched particulars will be mentioned. The particulars branched on the mentioned rule: We say: If he cuts a hand or a leg or a thigh or one-third of what is near the legs or less than half the head, then the cut-off part (muban) is haram, and the rest (muban minhu) is halal, because survival of life in the rest is expected (mutawahham). If he cuts it into two halves or three parts or most of what is near the rump (ajuz) or half the head or more, then both the cut-off part and the rest are halal, because the rest is alive only in form, not legally, as no survival of life is expected after such a wound. When he cuts off a hand or foot or thigh or the animal's third which is attached to the limbs or less than half the head, then the cut part is haram and the rest is halal because survival of life in the rest is expected. And if he cuts it into two pieces or three pieces and the part attached to the lower trunk is cut off, or he cuts half its head or most of it, then both the cut part and the rest are halal because the rest is alive only in form, not legally, as no survival of life is expected after such a wound. Explanation: In all the first examples, hope of the animal's survival existed, so the animal was ruled halal and the limb haram. In all the second examples, there was no hope of survival, so both were ruled halal. The remaining details are apparent from the translation.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning from the chapter on hunting (Kitab al-Sayd). It establishes a general rule (qaidah kulliyyah) for determining the legal status of a limb that is cut from a hunted animal while it is still alive. The key distinction is between formal life (hayat surah) and legal life (hayat hukman). If the animal retains enough life after the cut that it could have survived if slaughtered normally (madhbuh), then the cut limb is haram (forbidden) because it died without proper slaughter, but the rest of the animal is halal because the animal died from the wound, which is permissible for hunting. If the injury is so severe that the animal cannot survive (e.g., cut in half, head mostly severed), then both the cut limb and the rest are halal, because the animal is considered legally dead at the moment of the cut (hayat surah only). The passage also addresses a question from Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the relationship between the animal and its limb (principal/subordinate). The Hanafi response explains that subordination ceases when separation occurs. Examples illustrate the rule: if a limb is cut from an animal that could heal, the limb is haram; if the injury is fatal, both parts are halal. This qaidah is then applied to specific cases involving hands, feet, thighs, head portions, etc.

Key Terms
مبان (mubān): The part (limb) that is cut off from the animal.
مبان منه (mubān minhu): The animal from which the limb is cut.
حیوة صورة (hayāt ṣūrah): Life in appearance/formal sense, not sufficient for legal validity.
حیوة حكما (hayāt ḥukman): Legal life, i.e., life that carries legal consequences (e.g., meat is halal only if animal dies by proper slaughter or hunting).
توهم (tawahhum): Expectation or likelihood (of survival).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a hunter cuts a deer's leg (which is not fatal) and the deer dies from the wound, what is the ruling on the cut leg?
A) The leg is halal because the deer was alive when cut.
B) The leg is haram because the deer could have survived the leg cut.
C) The leg is halal because it is subordinate to the deer.
D) The leg is haram because it was not slaughtered properly.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules based on the expectation of survival (tawahhum) when a limb is cut from a hunted animal: if survival is expected, the limb is haram; if not, both are halal.

Ruling on a Limb Cut but Not Severed
Ruling statement on a partially severed limb from a hunted animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling for a partially severed limb on a hunted animal by evaluating the likelihood of healing, and explain the underlying principle of consideration of meanings.

Core Idea
A partially severed limb is halal only if healing is possible; otherwise, it is legally severed and haram.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario of hunting: when an arrow cuts a limb but the limb remains attached by skin. The ruling hinges on whether there is hope of the wound healing. This exemplifies the Hanafi principle that legal rulings consider underlying realities, not mere appearances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two scenarios
Recognize that a hunter strikes a game animal, cutting a limb without complete separation (hanging by skin). Two sub-cases exist: (1) healing is expected, (2) no hope of healing.
The key difference is the possibility of al-iltiyam (healing/joining).
2
Apply the principle 'al-'ibra li al-ma'ani'
Understanding that legal rulings are based on the real meaning and outcome, not just outward form. Here, if healing is possible, the limb is still part of the animal in meaning; if not, it is separated in meaning.
This is a core Hanafi legal maxim.
3
Derive the ruling for each scenario
If healing is possible, the cut limb is like the rest of the body, so the entire animal is halal. If healing is impossible, the limb is considered muban (severed) in meaning, making that limb haram while the rest is halal.
The ruling changes based on medical expectation.
4
Confirm the fatwa position
This is the standard Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah) applied in practice. The mufti should assess the likelihood of healing based on the nature of the wound and animal.
Uncertainty should be resolved by defaulting to the more cautious ruling? The passage doesn't specify, but typically if uncertain, one leans towards the default of halal unless sure of no healing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار کے بدن کا وہ حصہ جو الگ نہیں ہوا اور کٹ گیا اگر اس کے بھر جانے کا تو ہم ہو تو وہ حلال ہے
ولو ضرب صيدا فقطع يدا اورجلا ولم يبنه ان كان يتوهم الالتيام والاندمال فاذا مات حل اكله لانه بمنزلة سائر اجزائه وان كان لا يتوهم بان بقى متعلقا بجلده حل ما سواه لوجودا لا بانة معنى والعبرة للمعاني
ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے شکار کو مارا پس ہاتھ یا پیر کاٹ دیا اور اس کو جدا نہیں کیا تو اگر تو ہم مل جانے اور درست ہو جانے کا ہو پس جب وہ

That part of the hunted animal which is not separated but cut: if there is hope of it healing, then it is halal. And if he strikes a hunted animal and cuts off a hand or leg but does not separate it (cut it off completely), then if there is hope of it healing and joining (al-iltiyam wa al-indimal), then when it dies, eating it is halal because it is like the rest of its parts. If there is no hope, meaning it remains attached by its skin (bi-jildihi), then what is other than it (i.e., the rest of the animal) is halal, because in meaning (ma'nan), separation (ibanah) exists, and consideration is given to meanings. Translation: If he strikes a hunted animal and cuts off a hand or leg but does not separate it, if there is hope of it healing and becoming whole, then when it dies, eating it is halal, because it is like the rest of its parts. If there is no hope, meaning it remains hanging by its skin, then what is other than it is halal because, in meaning, separation has occurred, and consideration is for meanings. Explanation: If the hunter strikes the animal in such a place that the hand or leg is cut but hangs by skin, if there is hope of healing and recovery, then when the animal dies, the entire animal including the cut part is halal. If there is no hope of healing, then the cut part is haram and the rest is halal, because in meaning it is severed, and meanings are considered, not mere appearances.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on a limb that is cut but not completely severed from the animal (still attached by skin). The key factor is whether there is hope (tawahhum) of the wound healing (al-iltiyam wa al-indimal). If healing is possible, the cut part is still considered part of the animal; when the animal dies from the original wound, the entire animal is halal. If healing is impossible (the part remains hanging by skin), then the cut part is considered, in legal meaning (ma'nan), as already separated (muban). Therefore, that part is haram (like a cut limb), but the rest of the animal is halal. This is based on the principle that legal consideration is given to meanings (al-'ibrah li al-ma'ani). The outward appearance (still attached) is not decisive.

Key Terms
التيام (al-iltiyām): The healing and closing of a wound.
إبانة (ibānah): Separation of a limb; legally, if separation is certain in meaning (even if not completely detached), the ruling of muban applies.
العبرة للمعاني (al-'ibra li al-ma'ānī): Legal consideration is given to underlying meanings and realities, not mere external forms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Severed Parts: Halal When Survival Impossible
Explains when both severed part and body are halal based on tawahhum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on severed parts from a hunted animal and apply the condition of tawahhum (presumption of survival) to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
A severed limb is halal only if the animal could not have survived the injury – no presumption of life.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding parts of a hunted animal that become separated due to injury. The key factor is whether there is any realistic chance that the animal could survive the injury. Understanding this principle helps you properly apply the laws of halal consumption in complex hunting scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the ruling
The passage distinguishes between cases where survival is presumed (tawahhum exists) and where it is not. In the first case, the main body is halal but the severed limb is haram; in the second, both are halal.
2
Define the key term: tawahhum
Tawahhum means a reasonable presumption or expectation that the animal could remain alive after the injury. It is not certainty but a likelihood based on the nature and severity of the injury.
3
Apply the condition to given examples
In the examples (cutting into two or three pieces, cutting off half the head), there is no tawahhum because survival is impossible. Hence both the severed part and the main body are halal.
4
Contrast with cases where survival is possible
If the animal is injured but can survive, the severed limb is considered dead meat (haram), while the main body requires proper slaughter to be halal. This distinction is based on the presence of tawahhum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصيد
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴ حلال ہے اس لئے کہ باقی میں حیواۃ کے باقی رہنے کا تو ہم ہے اور اگر اس کے دوٹکڑے کر دیئے یا تین ٹکڑے کر دیئے اور جو حصہ نیچے کئے دھڑ سے متصل ہے وہ اگر کاٹ دیا یا اس کا آدھا سر یا اس میں اکثر کاٹ دیا تو مبان اور مبان منہ دونوں حلال ہیں اس لئے کہ مبان منہ صورۂ زندہ ہے نہ کہ حکما اس لئے کہ اس زخم کے بعد حیات کے باقی رہنے کا تو ھم نہیں ہے۔ تشریح یہ قاعدہ مذکورہ پر تفرع ہونے والی جزئیات میں پہلی تمام امثلہ میں شکار کے زندہ رہنے کا تو ھم تھا اسلئے شکار کو حلال اور عضو کو حرام قرار دیا اور دوسری تمام امثلہ میں اس کو تو ہم نہیں ہے کہ وہ زندہ رہ سکے تو یہاں دونوں کو حلال قرارد یا باقی تفصیل ترجمہ سے ظاہر ہے۔
Book of Hunting. Ashraf ul-Hidayah, Urdu commentary on Hidayah, Volume 14: [It] is halal because there is a presumption of survival remaining. And if one cuts it into two pieces or three pieces, and the part attached to the body that was cut below, or if one cuts its half head or most of it, then the separated [part] and the separated [part] are both halal, because the separated [part] is alive in form but not in ruling, because after this wound there is no presumption of remaining life. Explanation: This rule branches from the aforementioned rule. In the first set of examples, there was a presumption of the hunted animal remaining alive, so the hunted animal was declared halal and the limb haram. In the second set of examples, there is no presumption that it can remain alive, so here both are declared halal. The rest of the detail is evident from the translation.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a hunted animal that is cut into pieces. The key principle is whether there is a presumption (توهم) that the animal could survive after the injury. If such a presumption exists, the main body becomes halal only if properly slaughtered, but the severed limb is haram because it is considered a separate dead part. However, if the injury is so severe that survival is impossible (no presumption), then both the severed part and the main body are halal. The commentary explains that this is a branch of a previously mentioned rule, distinguishing between cases where hope of survival exists (first set of examples) and where it does not (second set). This is a typical Hanafi approach of considering effective cause ('illa) in rulings.

Key Terms
مبان (muban): The part that is severed or separated from the animal's body.
تو هم (tawahhum): Presumption or expectation that the animal could remain alive after injury.

Key Takeaway
A severed limb from a hunted animal is halal only when there is no realistic chance of the animal surviving the injury.

Exception for Fish: Severed Part is Halal
States exception for fish: severed part is halal despite being dead

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a severed part of a fish and explain the takhsis (exception) of the general hadith by the specific hadith.

Core Idea
A fish's severed part is halal because the specific exception overrides the general rule.

This lesson addresses an apparent conflict between two hadiths: the general rule that any part cut from a living animal is dead (and thus haram) and the specific permission that fish carrion is halal. The Hanafi school resolves this by applying the principle of takhsis — the specific hadith excludes fish from the general ruling. We will examine the proofs and the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the General Rule
Identify the general hadith 'ما أبين من الحی فهو ميت' and its ruling: any part severed from a living animal is considered dead carrion (maytah), which is haram for land animals.
2
Identify the Specific Exception
Recall the specific hadith that fish carrion (including severed parts) is halal. This hadith provides an exception.
3
Recognize the Conflict
Note that the general hadith includes fish, so a severed fish part would normally be considered dead carrion. But the specific hadith contradicts that.
4
Apply Takhsis (Specification)
Apply the usul principle that a specific text (khass) restricts the general text (amm). The specific hadith on fish carrion excludes fish from the general rule.
5
Derive the Final Ruling
Conclude that a severed part of a fish is halal, because fish carrion is halal by specific dalil, and the general rule does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مچھلی کا کٹا ہوا جز بھی حلال ہے
والحديث وان تناول السمك وما ابين منه فهو ميت الا ان ميتته حلال بالحديث الذي رويناه ترجمہ اور حدیث مذکور اگر چہ مچھلی کو شامل ہے اور مچھلی سے جدا کیا جائے پس وہ مردہ ہے مگر مچھلی کا میتہ حلال ہے اس حدیث کی وجہ سے کہ ہم روایت کر چکے ہیں۔ تشریح حدیث میں جو آیا ہے ما ابين من الحی فہو میت مچھلی کے لئے بھی عام ہے لہذا مچھلی سے جو عضو الگ کر لیا جائے وہ بھی میت ہوگا لیکن کیا کیا جائے کہ وہ میت ہونے کے باوجود بھی حلال ہو گا اس لئے کہ ماقبل میں حدیث گذر چکی ہے کہ اس کا میتہ حلال ہے اس لئے یہ حکم یہاں جاری نہ ہوگا۔
The cut-off part of fish is also halal. And the hadith: 'Whatever is cut off from a living animal is dead' includes fish, and that which is cut off from it is dead, except that its dead (carrion) is halal due to the hadith we narrated. Translation: And the mentioned hadith, although it includes fish, and what is separated from fish is dead, but the dead of fish is halal because of the hadith we have narrated. Explanation: The hadith 'Whatever is cut off from a living animal is dead' is general and includes fish. Therefore, a limb separated from fish would also be dead. But what to do? Even though it is dead, it will still be halal because earlier a hadith has passed that its dead is halal. Therefore, this ruling (the general ruling of the hadith) will not apply here.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage provides an exception to the general rule that severed parts of living animals are dead and thus haram. The general hadith 'Whatever is cut off from a living animal is dead' applies to fish as well, but fish has a special exemption: its dead (even without slaughter) is halal based on another hadith. Therefore, a severed part of a fish is halal to eat. This is a clear example of a specific proof (takhsis) overriding a general ruling.

Key Terms
میتہ (maytah): An animal that dies without proper Islamic slaughter; carrion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that a severed part of fish is halal is based on the usul principle of takhsis: a specific hadith exempts fish from the general rule that severed parts are dead carrion.

Hitting a Goat on the Neck: Ruling and Dislike
Ruling on hitting goat's neck, severing head, and hitting from back

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on slaughter by hitting the neck and distinguish between valid and invalid scenarios based on the cutting of the veins.

Core Idea
Meat is halal if veins are cut, even if the head is severed; hitting from the back requires the animal to be alive for vein cutting.

This passage addresses a practical scenario in slaughter: what happens if the neck is struck instead of a clean cut. You will learn that the key factor is whether the four veins (awdaj) are cut, and that certain methods are disliked or even invalid if the animal dies prematurely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and separate the two cases: one where the blow is to the front of the neck (severing the head), and one where the blow is from the back of the head (qafa).
The text uses 'ضرب عنق شاة' (hitting the neck) and 'ضربه من قبل القفا' (hitting from the back of the head).
2
Extract the ruling for each scenario
For the first scenario: the goat is halal because the veins are cut, but the action is disliked (makruh) because it reaches the spinal cord. For the second scenario: if the animal dies before the veins are cut, it is not halal; if it remains alive until the veins are cut, it is halal.
The condition for halal is cutting the awdaj (at least three of the four veins).
3
Understand the basis for dislike and invalidity
The dislike (karahah) in the first scenario is due to reaching the spinal cord (nukhā‘), which is not a direct invalidator but is discouraged. In the second scenario, the invalidity arises because the animal died before proper slaughter, making it carrion.
Reaching the spinal cord does not nullify the halal status; it only affects the method's desirability.
4
Synthesize the principle
The determining factor for halal meat is the cutting of the slaughter veins while the animal is alive. The method of reaching the veins (blow or cut) may affect the ruling if the animal dies before the veins are cut.
This principle is consistent with the broader Hanafi rule that slaughter must involve cutting the veins of the neck.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بکری کی گردن پر مارا اور سر الگ ہو گیا تو یہ بکری حلال ہے
ولو ضرب عنق شاة فابان راسها يحل لقطع الا وداج ويكره هذا الصنيع لا بلاغه النخاع وان ضربه من قبل القفا ان مات قبل قطع الأوداج لا يحل وان لم يمت حتى قطع الأوداج حل
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے بکری کی گردن پر مارا پس اس کا سر الگ کر دیا تو حلال ہے رگوں کے کٹنے کی وجہ سے اور یہ طریقہ مکروہ ہے اس کے نخاع تک پہنچا دینے کی وجہ سے اور اگر اس کو گدی کی طرف سے مارا تو اگر وہ رگیں کٹنے سے پہلے مر جائے تو حلال نہیں ہے اور اگر نہ مرے یہاں تک کہ رگیں کٹ جائیں تو حلال ہے۔ تشریح اگر بکری کی گردن ماری اور سرا الگ کر دیا تو یہ طریقہ مکروہ ہے اور بکری حلال ہے حلت کی وجہ ذبح کی رگوں کا کٹ جانا ہے اور کراہت کی نخاع تک پہنچانا ہے جس کا بیان کتاب الذبائح میں گذر چکا ہے اور اگر گدی کے پیچھے سے گردن ماری تو اگر رگیں کٹنے سے پہلے وہ مر جائے تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے اور اگر رگیں کٹنے تک وہ زندہ رہے تو حلال ہے۔
If one hits a goat's neck and the head becomes separated, this goat is halal. And if one strikes the neck of a goat and severs its head, it is halal due to the cutting of the veins, but this action is disliked because it reaches the spinal cord. And if one strikes it from the back of the head, if it dies before the cutting of the veins, it is not halal; and if it does not die until the veins are cut, it is halal. Translation: And if someone struck a goat's neck and thus separated its head, then it is halal because the veins are cut, and this method is disliked because it reaches the spinal cord. And if he struck it from the back of the head, then if it dies before the cutting of the veins, it is not halal; and if it does not die until the veins are cut, it is halal. Explanation: If one hits a goat's neck and separates the head, this method is disliked but the goat is halal. The reason for halal is the cutting of the slaughter veins, and the reason for dislike is reaching the spinal cord, as explained in the Book of Slaughterings. And if one strikes the neck from behind the head, then if it dies before the veins are cut, it is not halal; but if it remains alive until the veins are cut, it is halal.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage clarifies two scenarios of slaughtering a goat by hitting. First, if a blow to the neck separates the head, the meat is halal as long as the essential veins (wadaj) are cut. However, such a method is makruh tanzihan if it damages the spinal cord. Second, if the blow is from the back of the head (qafa), the animal must be alive and the veins cut after the blow. If the animal dies before the veins are cut, it becomes carrion and is haram. The Halal relies on the cutting of the veins, not the blow itself.

Key Terms
اوداج (awdāj): The four veins in the neck that must be cut for halal slaughter.
نخاع (nukhā‘): The spinal cord; reaching it during slaughter is makruh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person hits a goat on the back of the head and the goat dies before the veins are cut, what is the ruling?
A) Halal, because the blow is a form of slaughter
B) Halal, but makruh
C) Haram, because the animal died without proper slaughter
D) Halal only if the head is completely severed

Key Takeaway
The halal status of slaughtered meat depends on the cutting of the slaughter veins while the animal is alive; hitting the neck may be disliked but does not invalidate if the veins are cut.

Ruling on a Severed Limb Still Attached to the Body
Differentiates between attached part with healing hope and without

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a cut limb that remains attached by skin, and apply the criterion of healing presumption to determine its legal status.

Core Idea
A limb that cannot heal is legally severed even if physically attached — meaning governs form.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in hunting: a limb deeply cut but still attached. The Hanafi school distinguishes between cases where healing is possible and where it is not. The key principle is that legal meanings override physical appearances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two possibilities: (a) the wound is expected to heal, and (b) healing is not expected.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
If healing expected, the limb is like the rest of the body; both are halal after proper slaughter. If no healing, the limb is considered separate and its consumption is haram, though the rest of the animal is halal.
3
Understand the legal principle
The text explicitly states: 'العبرة للمعاني' – consideration is given to meanings, not appearances. Even though the limb is physically attached, the absence of healing potential gives it the meaning of a severed part.
4
Apply to a similar case
Think of a scenario where an animal is struck and a leg is cut but hanging by a thin skin: ruling depends on whether the leg can heal or not.

شکار کے بدن کا وہ حصہ جو الگ نہیں ہوا اور کٹ گیا اگر اس کے بھر جانے کا تو ہم ہو تو وہ حلال ہے
ولو ضرب صيدا فقطع يدا اورجلا ولم يبنه ان كان يتوهم الالتيام والاندمال فاذا مات حل اكله لانه بمنزلة سائر اجزائه وان كان لا يتوهم بان بقى متعلقا بجلده حل ما سواه لوجودا لا بانة معنى والعبرة للمعاني
ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے شکار کو مارا پس ہاتھ یا پیر کاٹ دیا اور اس کو جدا نہیں کیا تو اگر تو ہم مل جانے اور درست ہو جانے کا ہو پس جب وہ شکار مر جائے تو اس کا کھانا حلال ہے اس لئے کہ یہ اس کے تمام اجزاء کے درجہ میں ہے اور اگر اس کا تو ہم نہ ہو اس طریقہ پر کہ وہ لٹکا باقی رہ گیا اس کی کھال سے تو اس عضو کے علاوہ حلال ہے معنی جدائیگی کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے اور اعتبار معانی کا ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح شکار کے بدن کا جو حصہ الگ نہیں ہوا اور کٹ گیا تو اگر اس کے بھر جانے کا تو ہم ہو تو وہ باقی دیگر اجزاء کے حکم میں ہے شکار کے مرنے کے بعد یہ عضو اور شکار حلال ہے اور اگر وہ دیسے ہی کھال میں لٹکا رہ گیا ہے اور اس کے بھرنے کا تو ہم نہ ہو تو اس کو الگ شمار کیا جائے گا اور اس کا کھانا حلال نہ ہوگا البتہ شکار حلال ہو گا جب عضوا بھی متصل ہے تو آپ نے منفصل کیسے مان لیا ؟ جواب معنی منفصل ہی ہے منا ظاہر اور معانی ہی کا اعتبار ہوا کرتا ہے۔
That part of the hunted animal's body which is not separated but is cut: if there is a presumption of its healing and mending, then it is halal. And if one strikes a hunted animal and cuts a hand or foot but does not sever it, if there is presumption of healing and mending, then when the animal dies, eating it is halal because it is like the rest of its parts. And if there is no such presumption, and it remains hanging by its skin, then what is other than that part is halal because the meaning of separation is found, and consideration is given to meanings. Explanation: The part of the hunted animal's body that is not separated but cut: if there is a presumption of healing, then it is like the other parts; after the death of the hunted animal, this limb and the hunted animal are halal. And if it remains hanging by its skin and there is no presumption of healing, then it will be considered separate and eating it will not be halal, but the hunted animal will be halal. If the limb is still attached, how did you consider it separate? Answer: It is separate in meaning, and consideration is given to meanings, not appearances.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage deals with a limb that is deeply cut but still attached by skin. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes based on the likelihood of healing. If the wound is such that healing is expected, the limb is considered part of the living body, so when the animal is properly slaughtered, both body and limb are halal. However, if healing is impossible and the limb is only hanging by skin, it is deemed effectively separated (in meaning, not form). Therefore, it is treated like a severed part and is haram, though the rest of the animal is halal. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of giving weight to legal meanings over physical appearance.

Key Terms
التیام (iltiyām): Healing, mending of a wound.
اندمال (indimāl): Scarring over, complete healing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, when is a limb that is cut but still attached by skin considered haram to eat?
A) When it is completely severed from the body
B) When there is no presumption of healing
C) When the animal dies before slaughter
D) Always, because it is a severed part

Key Takeaway
The legal status of a partially severed limb depends on whether healing is expected, not on physical attachment alone.

Hunting by Majusi, Apostate, and Idolater
States that hunting by non-Ahl al-Kitab is impermissible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on hunting by a Majusi, apostate, or idolater and explain why it differs from hunting by a Jew or Christian.

Core Idea
Only those eligible for slaughter can make hunted game halal.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from al-Quduri’s matn that links the permissibility of hunting to the eligibility for Islamic slaughter (dakāh). The text states that the game of a Majusi, apostate, or idolater is not eaten, but the game of a Jew or Christian is permissible. We will explore the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is eating hunted game by a Majusi, apostate, or idolater. The ruling is that it is not permissible (haram).
2
Identify the exception
Jews and Christians are exceptions; their hunted game is halal despite not being Muslim.
3
Understand the rationale
The rationale is eligibility for slaughter (ahl al-dakāh). Muslims, Jews, and Christians are eligible; others are not.
4
Apply to a mixed parentage case
The note explains that a child born to a Jew and a Majusi follows the better religion, so the child's slaughter is halal.
This clarifies the classification of the child.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجوسی ، مرتد اور روشنی کے شکار کا حکم
قال ولا يوكل صيد المجوسى والمرتد والوثنى لا نهم ليسوا من اهل الذكاة على ما بيناه في الذبائح ولا بدمنها في اباحة الصيد بخلاف النصراني واليهودى لا نهما من اهل الذكاة اختيارا فكذا اضطرارا ترجمہ قردری نے فرمایا اور مجوسی اور بت پرست کا شکار نہیں کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ یہ دونوں ذکاۃ کے اہل نہیں ہیں اس تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو ہم کتاب الذبائح میں بیان کر چکے ہیں اور اس لئے کہ شکار کی اباحت کیلئے ذکاۃ ہونا ضروری ہے بخلاف نصرانی اور یہودی کے اس لئے کہ وہ دونوں ذکاۃ ختیاری کے اہل ہیں پس ایسے ہی ذکاۃ اضطراری کے (اہل ہوں گے)۔ تشریح شکار میں زکاۃ الفطراری ایسی ہے جیسے غیر شکار میں ذکاۃ اختیاری تو جو ذ کا اختیاری کا اہل نہ ہوگا وہ شکار کرنے کا بھی اہل نہ ہوگا اور جو ذ کا اختیاری کا اہل ہوگا تو وہ ذکاۃ اضطراری اور شکار کرنے کا اہل ہوگا اور نصرانی و یہودی کا ذبیحہ حلال ہوگا۔ تنبیہ جو بچہ یہودی اور مجوسی سے پیدا ہوا ہے اس کا ذبیہ اور اس کا مارا ہوا شکار حلال ہے اس لئے کہ بچہ کو یہودی شمار کیا جائے گا کیونکہ بچہ والدین میں سے جس کا دین بہتر ہو اس کے دین کے تابع ہوا کرتا ہے۔
The ruling of hunting by a Majusi, apostate, and idolater. He (al-Quduri) said: The hunted game of a Majusi, apostate, and idolater is not eaten because they are not among the people of slaughter (ahl al-dhakah), as we explained in the Book of Slaughterings. And slaughter is necessary for the permissibility of hunting, unlike a Christian and a Jew, because they are among the people of voluntary slaughter, and thus also of emergency slaughter. Translation: Quduri said: The hunt of a Majusi and an idolater will not be eaten because these two are not eligible for slaughter according to the detail we explained in the Book of Slaughterings, and because slaughter is necessary for the permissibility of hunting, unlike a Christian and a Jew, because they are eligible for voluntary slaughter, so likewise they (will be eligible) for emergency slaughter. Explanation: In hunting, emergency slaughter is like voluntary slaughter in non-hunting. So whoever is not eligible for voluntary slaughter will not be eligible for hunting, and whoever is eligible for voluntary slaughter will be eligible for emergency slaughter and hunting. And the slaughtered animal of a Christian and a Jew is halal. Note: The child born to a Jew and a Majusi: his slaughter and his killed game are halal, because the child will be considered a Jew, since the child follows the better religion of the parents.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage establishes that the permissibility of hunting is tied to the eligibility for proper Islamic slaughter (dhabh). Only those who are Ahl al-Kitab (Jews and Christians) or Muslims can perform valid slaughter. Hunted game by a Majusi (Zoroastrian), apostate (murtad), or idolater (wathani) is not halal because they are not considered 'people of slaughter' (ahl al-dhakah). A child born to a Jewish parent and a Majusi parent is considered Jewish (following the better religion), so his slaughter is halal. This ruling is based on earlier discussions in the Book of Slaughterings.

Key Terms
ذکاۃ (dakāh): Islamic slaughter, which includes cutting the throat and letting blood.
مجوسی (majūsī): A Zoroastrian, fire-worshipper.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, whose hunted game is considered halal?
A) A Muslim only
B) A Muslim, Jew, or Christian
C) Any person who hunts
D) Only a Muslim or Jew

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of hunted game depends on the hunter's eligibility for proper Islamic slaughter; only Muslims, Jews, and Christians can perform valid slaughter, and thus their game is halal.

Ownership of Game: Wounding vs. Effective Capture
Determines ownership when first shooter wounds but second kills

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of a game when the first shooter wounds but the second kills, and evaluate the role of effective capture (akhdh) as the decisive criterion.

Core Idea
Ownership of game is established by effective capture, not merely by hitting first.

In hunting, the question arises: who owns the animal when two people shoot, one wounding and the other killing? The Hanafi school relies on the hadith 'الصيد لمن اخذ' to determine that the one who effectively takes possession, i.e., the one who removes the animal from the state of resistance (ḥayz al-imtinā‘) and kills it, becomes the owner. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is two people shooting the same animal. The Hanafi ruling: the animal belongs to the second shooter and is halal (permissible to eat) for him.
The first shooter only wounded the animal; it remained in ḥayz al-imtinā‘ (state of resistance).
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the hadith: 'الصيد لمن اخذ' (The game belongs to the one who takes it). This is a general proof (dalil ‘amm) from the Sunnah.
The hadith is a single transmission (khabar wahid), thus zanni al-thubut, but accepted in fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'لمن اخذ' (to the one who takes) indicates that ownership is tied to the act of taking (akhdh), not merely hitting or wounding. Effective capture is when the animal is removed from its ability to escape.
Compare with the first shooter: he did not 'take' (akhdh) because the animal retained the ability to resist.
4
Note the condition for ownership
The key condition is that the first shooter did not 'severely wound' (lam yuthkhin) and did not remove the animal from ḥayz al-imtinā‘. If he had, the animal would be his.
This condition is derived from the matn: 'لم يثخنه ولم يخرجه عن حيز الامتناع'.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
The second shooter, by killing and taking possession, becomes the owner. The first shooter has no claim. This ruling clarifies disputes in hunting.
The principle is general: any game that is not effectively captured after wounding remains open for others to claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص نے شکار کو تیر مارا اور اس شکار میں اپنے کو بچانے کی قوت موجود ہے اور وہ اپنے کو بچا سکتا ہے کہ دوسرے شخص نے تیر مارا اور اس کو ہلاک کر دیا یہ حلال ہے یا نہیں، اس کا مالک کون ہے؟
قال ومن رمى صيدا فاصابه ولم يثخنه ولم يخرجه عن حيز الامتناع فرماه اخر فقتله فهو للثاني يوكل لانه هو الاخذ وقد قال عليه السلام الصيد لمن اخذ
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس نے شکار کو تیر مارا پس وہ اسکوں گا اور اس کو سخت زخمی نہیں کیا اور اس کو چیز امتناع سے خارج نہیں کیا پس دوسرے شخص نے اس کو تیر مارا پس اس کو مار ڈالا تو شکار دوسرے کا ہوگا اور کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ دوسرا ہی اس کو پکڑنے والا ہے اور نبی علیہ السلام نے فرمایا ہے کہ شکار اس کے لئے ہوتا ہے جو اس کو گرفتار کرے۔ تشریح ایک شخص نے شکار کو تیر مارا اور وہ لگا لیکن وحشی جانور میں اپنے کو بچانے کی جو قوت ہوتی ہے وہ اس میں موجود ہے کہ وہ اپنے کو بچا سکتا ہے کہ دوسرے شخص نے اس کو تیر مارا اور اسکو ہلاک کر دیا اب یہاں دوسوال کھڑے ہو گئے
A person shot an arrow at a hunted animal, and that hunted animal has the ability to defend itself and can save itself, then another person shot an arrow and killed it. Is it halal or not? Who is its owner? He (al-Quduri) said: And whoever shot at a hunted animal and hit it but did not severely wound it and did not remove it from the state of resistance, then another person shot it and killed it, then it belongs to the second person and it is eaten, because he is the one who took it, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The hunted game belongs to the one who takes it.' Translation: Quduri said: And whoever shot at a hunted animal and hit it but did not severely wound it and did not remove it from the state of resistance, then another person shot it and killed it, then the hunted animal belongs to the second and will be eaten, because the second is the one who caught it, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The hunted game belongs to the one who takes it.' Explanation: A person shot an arrow at the hunted animal, and it hit, but the wild animal still has the ability to defend itself and can save itself. Then another person shot it and killed it. Now two questions arise here...
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where two people shoot the same animal. The first shooter wounds the animal but does not incapacitate it; the second shooter kills it. The Hanafi ruling, based on Quduri's matn and the hadith 'الصيد لمن اخذ', states that the animal belongs to the second person because he is the one who effectively captured it (اخذ). The first shooter did not achieve effective capture (لم يثخنه ولم يخرجه عن حيز الامتناع). Therefore, the game is halal for the second person. The commentary notes that this raises further questions, but the core ruling is clear.

Key Terms
حیز امتناع (ḥayz al-imtinā‘): The state of being able to resist or escape; the animal's capacity to avoid capture.
أخذ (akhdh): Taking possession, capturing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the decisive criterion for ownership of a hunted animal when two people shoot it?
A) Who shot first
B) Who inflicted the fatal wound
C) Who took possession (akhdh) after removing the animal from ḥayz al-imtinā‘
D) The animal belongs to the landowner

Key Takeaway
Ownership of game in Hanafi fiqh is determined by effective capture (akhdh), i.e., removing the animal from its state of resistance, not by who first wounds it.

Ruling on a Hunted Animal's Severed Limb Hanging by Skin
Explains ruling when a limb is severed but hangs by skin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a hunted animal's limb that is severed but remains attached by skin, and explain the basis in considering the effective cause (ma'na) over physical appearance.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a limb that hangs by skin is considered separate in meaning, so it is not halal, while the rest of the animal is halal.

When hunting, an animal may die with a limb partially severed yet still attached by skin. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling based on the underlying legal meaning (ma'na) rather than mere physical connection. This passage explains how the principle of considering effective causes determines the permissibility of consuming the limb versus the rest of the animal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two situations: (1) the limb is completely severed from the body; (2) the limb is cut but remains hanging by the skin. Recognize each as distinct for the ruling.
2
Note the ruling per scenario
State the ruling: In the first case, the limb is like the rest of the animal and is halal. In the second, only the hanging limb is not halal; the rest of the animal remains halal.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The difference hinges on the 'meaning of separation' (ma'na al-infiṣāl). Even if physically attached by skin, the effective cause (separation in meaning) governs the ruling. Hanafis give weight to the effective meaning over literal appearance.
This is a key usul principle: al-‘ibrah bi al-ma‘ānī.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
Consider other scenarios where partial attachment leaves doubt. The principle of considering ma'na helps determine whether an item is treated as separate or connected in legal rulings.
For example, a fruit partially cut from a tree but still attached by skin may have a similar ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار مر جائے تو اس کا کھانا حلال ہے اس لئے کہ یہ اس کے تمام اجزاء کے درجہ میں ہے اور اگر اس کا تو ہم نہ ہو اس طریقہ پر کہ وہ لٹکا باقی رہ گیا اس کی کھال سے تو اس عضو کے علاوہ حلال ہے معنی جدائیگی کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے اور اعتبار معانی کا ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح شکار کے بدن کا جو حصہ الگ نہیں ہوا اور کٹ گیا تو اگر اس کے بھر جانے کا تو ہم ہو تو وہ باقی دیگر اجزاء کے حکم میں ہے شکار کے مرنے کے بعد یہ عضو اور شکار حلال ہے اور اگر وہ دیسے ہی کھال میں لڑکا رہ گیا ہے اور اس کے بھرنے کا تو ہم نہ ہو تو اس کو الگ شمار کیا جائے گا اور اس کا کھانا حلال نہ ہوگا البتہ شکار حلال ہو گا جب عضوا بھی متصل ہے تو آپ نے منفصل کیسے مان لیا ؟ جواب معنی منفصل ہی ہے منا ظاہر اور معانی ہی کا اعتبار ہوا کرتا ہے۔
If the hunted [animal] dies, its meat is halal because it is in the status of all its parts. And if it is not [cut] entirely in such a way that it remains hanging by its skin, then apart from that limb it is halal due to the presence of the meaning of separation, and consideration is given to meanings.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explaining the status of a severed limb that remains attached by skin. The author clarifies that if a limb is completely severed, it is treated as part of the animal and is halal. However, if it is only partially severed and hangs by the skin, the limb itself is considered separate (in meaning) and is not halal, while the rest of the animal is halal. The reasoning is that legal consideration is given to the underlying meaning (separation) rather than mere physical appearance.

Key Terms
معنی (ma'na): The underlying legal meaning or effective cause, as opposed to literal appearance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunted animal's limb is severed but remains hanging by the skin, what is the ruling?
A) The limb is halal and the rest is haram
B) The limb is not halal but the rest is halal
C) The entire animal becomes haram
D) The entire animal is halal

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a limb that is effectively separated in meaning—even if physically attached by skin—is not halal, while the rest of the animal remains permissible.

Eligibility for Hunting: Non-Muslim Hunters and Dhakah
Ruling on hunting by non-Muslims and child of mixed parents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating game hunted by Majusis, apostates, and idolaters, and explain the legal basis involving eligibility for dhakah.

Core Idea
Only those eligible for voluntary dhakah may hunt; Majusis, apostates, and idolaters are excluded.

In this passage, the author from Quduri’s text clarifies a key distinction: not all non-Muslims are equal when it comes to hunting. The permissibility of eating game depends on the hunter's ability to perform Islamic slaughter (dhakah) voluntarily. Understanding this rule helps you apply the conditions for halal meat from non-Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The text names three types of hunters whose game is not eaten: Majusi (fire-worshipper), apostate, and idolater (wathani). Note that Christians and Jews are excluded from this prohibition.
Majusis are often Zoroastrians; 'wathani' refers to idol worshippers.
2
Recognize the legal reason (illah)
The prohibition is because these groups are not 'people of dhakah' (ahl al-dhakah). Dhakah is the Islamic method of slaughter required for permissibility.
The text references a previous explanation in the chapter on slaughter (al-dhaba'ih).
3
Distinguish between voluntary and involuntary dhakah
Voluntary dhakah is for domestic animals; hunting is a form of involuntary dhakah (idtirari). The same eligibility applies: one who can perform voluntary dhakah can also hunt.
This is drawn from the Urdu commentary: 'dhakah al-ikhtiyari' vs 'dhakah al-idtirari'.
4
Apply the child of mixed parents rule
A child born to a Jewish and a Majusi parent follows the better religion (Judaism), so his slaughter and hunting are halal.
The basis: the child is considered of the religion with the better status, thus ahl al-dhakah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا يوكل صيد المجوسى والمرتد والوثنى لا نهم ليسوا من اهل الذكاة على ما بيناه في الذبائح ولا بدمنها في اباحة الصيد بخلاف النصراني واليهودى لا نهما من اهل الذكاة اختيارا فكذا اضطرارا ترجمہ قردری نے فرمایا اور مجوسی اور بت پرست کا شکار نہیں کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ یہ دونوں ذکاۃ کے اہل نہیں ہیں اس تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو ہم کتاب الذبائح میں بیان کر چکے ہیں اور اس لئے کہ شکار کی اباحت کیلئے ذکاۃ ہونا ضروری ہے بخلاف نصرانی اور یہودی کے اس لئے کہ وہ دونوں ذکاۃ ختیاری کے اہل ہیں پس ایسے ہی ذکاۃ اضطراری کے (اہل ہوں گے)۔ تشریح شکار میں زکاۃ الفطراری ایسی ہے جیسے غیر شکار میں ذکاۃ اختیاری تو جو ذ کا اختیاری کا اہل نہ ہوگا وہ شکار کرنے کا بھی اہل نہ ہوگا اور جو ذ کا اختیاری کا اہل ہوگا تو وہ ذکاۃ اضطراری اور شکار کرنے کا اہل ہوگا اور نصرانی و یہودی کا ذبیحہ حلال ہوگا۔ تنبیہ جو بچہ یہودی اور مجوسی سے پیدا ہوا ہے اس کا ذبیہ اور اس کا مارا ہوا شکار حلال ہے اس لئے کہ بچہ کو یہودی شمار کیا جائے گا کیونکہ بچہ والدین میں سے جس کا دین بہتر ہو اس کے دین کے تابع ہوا کرتا ہے۔
He said: The game of the Majusi, the apostate, and the idolater is not eaten because they are not among the people of dhakah as we explained in [the chapter of] slaughter, and dhakah is necessary for the permissibility of game, unlike the Christian and the Jew because they are among the people of dhakah voluntarily, so likewise involuntarily. [Urdu translation and commentary follow]
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the permissibility of eating game hunted by non-Muslims. The author, following Quduri, rules that game hunted by a Majusi (fire-worshipper), an apostate, or an idolater is not permissible to eat because they are not qualified to perform Islamic slaughter (dhakah). This is contrasted with Christians and Jews, who are considered people of the Book and are qualified to perform dhakah voluntarily; therefore, their hunting (which is a form of involuntary dhakah) is also permissible. A further note clarifies that a child born to a Jewish and a Majusi parent follows the better religion (Judaism), so his slaughter and hunting are halal.

Key Terms
ذكاة (dhakah): The Islamic method of slaughtering animals, required for permissibility of meat; includes voluntary (for domestic animals) and involuntary (for hunted game).
مجوسي (majusi): A follower of Zoroastrianism, considered not ahl al-kitab.
وثني (wathani): An idolater, one who worships idols.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because Majusis are not among the People of the Book
B) Because they are not qualified to perform dhakah
C) Because they do not believe in God
D) Because their hunting tools are impure

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of eating game depends on the hunter's eligibility for dhakah; only Muslims and People of the Book are eligible.

Ownership of Game When First Hunter Does Not Incapacitate
States the ruling and proof for shared hunting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on shared hunting to determine ownership based on effective seizure (akhdh).

Core Idea
Game belongs to the one who seizes it, not the one who wounds it, if the animal remains able to escape.

When two hunters target the same game, disputes over ownership arise. This passage from al-Quduri clarifies that mere wounding does not confer ownership unless the animal is incapacitated. The ruling hinges on the concept of 'taking' (akhdh) and the animal's state of resistance (hīz al-imtināʿ). Let's examine the ruling, its condition, and the prophetic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and condition
State the scenario: first hunter wounds, second kills. Identify the key condition: the animal must not be removed from the 'realm of resistance' (hīz al-imtināʿ) by the first hunter.
The condition determines who is the effective taker.
2
Apply the concept of effective seizure
Since the animal could still escape, it was not effectively seized by the first hunter. The second hunter, by killing it, completes the seizure. Ownership follows the taker.
3
Cite and analyze the proof
The Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'Game belongs to the one who takes it.' The operative word 'akhadha' means to seize with the intent of ownership. The second hunter is the actual taker.
The hadith is considered gharib (weak in transmission) but the ruling is established via other proofs or istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن رمى صيدا فاصابه ولم يثخنه ولم يخرجه عن حيز الامتناع فرماه اخر فقتله فهو للثاني يوكل لانه هو الاخذ وقد قال عليه السلام الصيد لمن اخذ ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس نے شکار کو تیر مارا پس وہ اسکوں گا اور اس کو سخت زخمی نہیں کیا اور اس کو چیز امتناع سے خارج نہیں کیا پس دوسرے شخص نے اس کو تیر مارا پس اس کو مار ڈالا تو شکار دوسرے کا ہوگا اور کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ دوسرا ہی اس کو پکڑنے والا ہے اور نبی علیہ السلام نے فرمایا ہے کہ شکار اس کے لئے ہوتا ہے جو اس کو گرفتار کرے۔ تشریح ایک شخص نے شکار کو تیر مارا اور وہ لگا لیکن وحشی جانور میں اپنے کو بچانے کی جو قوت ہوتی ہے وہ اس میں موجود ہے کہ وہ اپنے کو بچا سکتا ہے کہ دوسرے شخص نے اس کو تیر مارا اور اسکو ہلاک کر دیا اب یہاں دوسوال کھڑے ہو گئے تو جواب دیا کہ شکار حلال ہے اس لئے کہ وہ ابھی صید ہونے سے خارج نہیں ہوا تھا اس سے پہلے ہی دوسرے نے اس کو تیر مار دیا تو تیر شکار کو لگا جہاں ذکاۃ اضطراری کافی ہوتی ہے اور اس کا مالک دوسرا شخص ہے کیونکہ پکڑنے والا اور اس پر قبضہ کرنے والا دوسرا ہی مشخص ہے اور حدیث میں ہے کہ شکار اسی کا ہوتا ہے جو اس کو پکڑے اور پکڑنے والا دوسرا ہے حدیث مذکور غریب ہے جس کی کوئی اصل نہیں ہے۔
He said: Whoever shoots game and hits it but does not severely wound it and does not take it out of the realm of resistance, then another shoots it and kills it, then it belongs to the second and is eaten because he is the one who took it, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Game belongs to the one who takes it.' [Urdu translation and commentary follow]
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling when two hunters are involved with the same game. The first hunter shoots and wounds the animal, but the animal remains capable of escaping. The second hunter then shoots and kills it. According to the text, the game belongs to the second hunter and is permissible to eat. This is based on the hadith that game belongs to the one who takes it (al-sayd liman akhadhahu). The commentary notes that this hadith is gharib (weak/unauthentic), but the ruling is still established. The underlying reasoning is that the first hunter did not effectively seize the animal, as it remained in the state of resistance (hīz al-imtināʿ).

Key Terms
حيز الامتناع (hīz al-imtināʿ): The state of being able to avoid capture; the animal's ability to escape.
الأخذ (al-akhdh): Taking or seizing with the intention of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key condition that determines ownership of game when two hunters are involved?
A) Which hunter fired first.
B) Which hunter caused the fatal wound.
C) Whether the animal was removed from the realm of resistance by the first hunter.
D) Whether the first hunter intended to kill.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of game depends on effective seizure (akhdh), not merely wounding; an animal in the realm of resistance is not considered taken.

Understanding Hīz al-Imtināʿ (Realm of Resistance)
Explains the key condition in the hunting ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hīz al-imtināʿ and explain its role in determining ownership in hunting law.

Core Idea
An animal in the realm of resistance retains legal freedom; only incapacitation transfers ownership.

The term hīz al-imtināʿ appears in this hunting ruling as the key condition. It refers to the animal's ability to escape or avoid capture. Understanding this concept is essential for correctly applying the Hanafi law of hunting and seizure. This lesson unpacks the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Define hīz al-imtināʿ
Literal meaning: sphere of resistance. Legal meaning: the state where an animal can still evade capture.
Contrast with incapacity (like severe wound or capture).
2
Identify its legal effect
As long as the animal is in this state, it is not considered 'taken' by any hunter. Only when it is removed from this state (by incapacitation or direct capture) does ownership transfer.
3
Apply to the scenario
In the passage, the first hunter wounds but does not remove the animal from hīz al-imtināʿ. Therefore, the second hunter's kill counts as a fresh taking.
If the first had removed it, he would own it.

Ruling When First Hunter Incapacitates Game and Second Kills
Explains ruling when first hunter severely wounds game, then second kills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a first hunter incapacitates game and a second kills it, and distinguish when the meat is halal versus haram.

Core Idea
Incapacitated game must be slaughtered; the second's kill is invalid unless the first shot is fatal.

Hunting involves releasing an arrow or bullet at game, but when two hunters are involved, ownership and halal status can become complex. This lesson addresses the scenario where the first hunter's shot severely wounds the animal, and the second hunter delivers the killing blow. The key is whether the first hunter had the ability to perform proper slaughter (dhakah ikhtiyari) after incapacitating the game.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subcases
The passage discusses two distinct scenarios: (1) the first shot incapacitates the game but it could still escape, and (2) the first shot leaves no more life than in a properly slaughtered animal. Identify which case leads to halal meat.
The distinction hinges on the remaining life after the first shot.
2
Understand the ruling for the normal incapacitation case
When the first hunter wounds the game but it could survive, the animal belongs to the first hunter but is not permissible to eat. Reason: the second hunter's shot is not valid dhakah because the first had the ability to perform voluntary slaughter.
The first hunter's failure to slaughter properly makes the second's kill ineffective.
3
Understand the exception for the fatal first shot
If the first shot is so severe that the animal has no more life than a slaughtered animal (e.g., head severed), then the game is halal and belongs to the first hunter. Death is attributed solely to the first shot, and the second shot is irrelevant.
The second shot's existence or non-existence is the same; it does not affect death.
4
Apply the principle of ownership
In both subcases, ownership goes to the first hunter because he initiated the incapacitation. However, permissibility to eat depends on the severity of the first shot.
Ownership and permissibility are decided separately.
5
Summarize the epistemological weight
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, the core Hanafi texts. It is relied upon in fatwa.
Authority weight: zahir_al_riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان كان الاول تخنـه فـرمـاه الثاني فقتله فهو للاول ولم يوكل لاحتمال الموت بالثاني وهو ليس بذكاة للقدرة على ذكاة الاختيار بخلاف الوجه الأول وهذا اذا كان الرمى الأول بحال ينجو منه الصيد لانه حينئذ يكون الموت مضافا الى الرمى الثانى اما اذا كان الأول بحال لا يسلم منه الصيد بان لا يبقى فيه من الحيوة الا بقدر ما يبقى فى المذبوح كما اذا بان راسه يحل لان الموت لا يضاف الى الرمي الثاني لان وجوده وعدمه بمنزلة ترجمہ اور اگر پہلے نے اس کو سخت زخمی کر دیا پس دوسرے نے اس کو تیر مارا پس اس کو مارڈالا تو شکار پہلے کا ہے اورکھایا نہیں جائے گا دوسرے تیر سے موت کے احتمال کی وجہ سے اور یہ ذکاۃ نہیں ہے ذکاۃ اختیاری پر قدرت کی وجہ سے بخلاف پہلی صورت کے اور یہ جب ہے جبکہ پہلا تیر پھینکنا ایسی حالت پر ہو کہ جس نے شکار بچ سکے گا اس لئے کہ اس وقت موت دوسرے تیر پھینکنے کی طرف منسوب ہوگی بہر حال جبکہ اول ایسی حالت پر ہو کہ جس سے شکار صحیح سالم رہ سکے اس طریقہ پر کہ اس میں اتنی حیات باقی ہو جتنی مذبوح میں باقی ہوتی ہے جیسے اس کا سر جدا کر دیا ہو تو حلال ہے اس لئے کہ موت دوسرے تیر پھینکنے کی جانب منسوب نہ ہوگی اس لئے کہ ثانی کا وجود اور عدم ایک درجہ
And if the first severely wounded it, then the second shot it and killed it, then it belongs to the first and is not eaten due to the possibility of death by the second, and it is not a dhakah because of the ability to perform voluntary dhakah, unlike the first case. This is when the first shot was such that the game could escape from it, because then death is attributed to the second shot. But if the first was such that the game could not survive from it, such that no more life remains than what remains in a slaughtered animal, as if its head was severed, then it is halal because death is not attributed to the second shot, since the second's existence and non-existence are the same.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where the first hunter's shot is so severe that the animal is incapacitated and can no longer escape. In this case, the game belongs to the first hunter, but it is not permissible to eat because the death of the animal could have been caused by the second hunter's shot, and the first hunter had the ability to perform proper slaughter (dhakah ikhtiyari) but did not do so. Therefore, the second hunter's shot is not considered a valid dhakah. However, there is an exception: if the first hunter's shot was so devastating that the animal had no more life than a properly slaughtered animal (e.g., its head was cut off), then the game is halal and belongs to the first hunter, because death is attributed solely to the first shot, and the second shot is irrelevant.

Key Terms
ذكاة اختیاری (dhakah ikhtiyari): Voluntary slaughter performed on an animal that is under one's control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if hunter A shoots a deer and severs its head, then hunter B shoots it, what is the ruling?
A) The deer belongs to A and is halal
B) The deer belongs to B and is halal
C) The deer belongs to A but is haram
D) The deer is ownerless

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of eating game shot by a second hunter depends on whether the first hunter could have performed voluntary slaughter; if so, the meat is haram unless the first shot was already fatal.

Liability of Two Hunters on Shared Game Death
States ruling from al-Ziyādāt on liability when game dies from two wounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three components of compensation (ḍamān) owed by the second hunter when a hunted animal dies from wounds inflicted by two hunters, and explain the legal reasoning (ta‘līl) behind each component.

Core Idea
Shared causation splits liability: second hunter guarantees damage, half value, half meat.

This passage from al-Ziyādāt by Imam Muḥammad al-Shaybānī presents a nuanced ruling on liability when two hunters wound an animal and it dies from the combined wounds. The second hunter must compensate the first hunter (the owner of the animal) for three distinct losses. The commentary unpacks the legal reasoning, showing how the Ḥanafī school apportions liability based on causation and prevents double compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three guarantees
Read the Arabic ruling: 'yadmanu al-thānī mā naqaṣathu jirāḥatuhu thumma yadmanu niṣfa qīmatihi majrūḥan bi-jirāḥatayni thumma yadmanu niṣfa qīmat al-laḥmi'. List the three items the second hunter must pay.
The three are: damage (nuqsān), half value (niṣf qīma), half meat (niṣf laḥm).
2
Examine the ta‘līl for each
The commentary gives reasoning for each: (1) the wound directly reduces value; (2) death from both wounds means he destroyed half; (3) the first shot made it potentially halal, second shot spoiled half the meat.
Understand the logic of attribution and avoidance of double counting.
3
Note how double compensation is prevented
The second hunter does not guarantee his wound again because it is already covered by the damage guarantee, and the meat guarantee is separate because it relates to the lost opportunity of halal slaughter.
The phrase 'dakhala ḍamān al-laḥm fīhi' indicates the meat guarantee is subsumed in the value guarantee for the other half.
4
Apply to a scenario
Work through a numerical example: if the animal's value after first wound is 80, after second wound is 60, meat value is 40. Calculate what the second hunter owes.
Damage = 20, half value = 30, half meat = 20; total = 70.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العيد
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴

وان علم ان الموت حصل من الجراحتين اولا يدرى قال فى الزيادات يضمن الثانى ما نقصته جراحته ثم يضمن نصف قيمته مجروحاً بجراحتين ثم يضمن نصف قيمة لحمه

اما الأول فلانه جرح حيوانا مملوكا للغير وقد نقصه فيضمن ما نقصه اولا واما الثاني فلان الموت حصل بالجراحتين فيكون هو متلفا نصفه وهو مملوک بغيره فيضمن نصف قيمته مجروحا بالجراحتين لان الأولى ما كانت بصنعه والثانية ضمنها مرة فلا يضمنها ثانيا

واما الثالث فلان بالرمى الاول صار بحال يحل بذكاة الاختيار لولارمى الثانى فهذا بالرمى الثانى افسد عليه نصف اللحم فيضمنه ولا يضمن النصف الأخر لانه ضمنه مرة فدخل ضمان اللحم فيه

تشریح صورت مذکورہ کا حکم تو مصنف نے زیادات کے حوالہ سے بیان کر دیا جس میں تین چیزیں ہیں
- ضمان نقصان
-۲- ضمان قیمت
-- ضمان قیمت لحم

یہاں سے مصنف ہر ایک کی دلیل فرماتے ہیں ضمان نقصان کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ دوسرے نے دوسرے کا مملوک شکار مجروح کبر کے اس کو ناقص کر دیا لہذا پہلے اس سے نقصان کا ضمان وصول کیا جائے گا۔

ضمان قیمت مجروح کی دلیل ... جب شکار کی موت دونوں کے زخم سے واقع ہوئی ہے تو دوسرے نے غیر کے مملوک شکار کے نصف کو تلف کر دیا ہے لہذا اس پر نصف قیمت واجب ہوگی مگر مجروح کی قیمت کا نصف ہوگا اور مجروح بھی وہ مجروح جو دو زخموں سے مجروح ہے جو مثال مذکور میں ۱۲ روپے تھے جس کا نصف ۶ روپے ہے۔

سوال دوسرے پر زخموں کا ضمان بھی واجب ہونا چاہیے؟
جواب پہلے کے زخم میں تو دوسرے کا کوئی دخل نہیں اس پر اس کا منمان بھی واجب نہ ہوگا اور رہا اس کا اپنا لگایا ہوا ز ثم تو اس کا وہ پہلے ضمان نقصان ادا کر چکا ہے تو اس کا دوبارہ ضمان اس پر واجب نہ ہوگا ۔

ضمان قیمت لحم کی دلیل
واما الثالث فلان بالرمى الاول صار بحال يحل بذكاة الاختيار لولارمى الثانى فهذا بالرمى الثانى افسد عليه نصف اللحم فيضمنه ولا يضمن النصف الأخر لانه ضمنه مرة فدخل ضمان اللحم فيه

ترجمہ اور بہر حال ثالث پس اس لئے کہ شکار پہلے تیر پھینکنے کی وجہ سے ایسے حال میں تھا کہ وہ ذکاۃ اختیاری سے حلال ہو جاتا اگر دوسرا تیر نہ پھینکتا تو دوسرے نے دوسرا تیر پھینک کر اول پر نصف گوشت کو فاسد کر دیا تو ثانی اس کا ضامن ہوگا اور دوسرے نصف کا ضامن نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ وہ ایک مرتبہ اس کا ضامن ہو چکا ہے تو اس میں گوشت کا منمان داخل ہو گیا۔

تشریح یہ گوشت کے نصف قیمت کے ضمان کی دلیل ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ پہلا تیر لگنے کے بعد اس میں اتنی حیات تھی کہ اس کو ذکاۃ اختیاری سے ذبح کیا جاتا اگر دوسرا تیر نہ مارتا۔
If it is known that death resulted from both wounds, or it is not known, Imam Muhammad said in al-Ziyadat: The second person guarantees what his wound diminished, then he guarantees half of its value while it is wounded with two wounds, then he guarantees half of the value of its meat. As for the first (guarantee), because he wounded an animal owned by another and diminished it, so he guarantees what he diminished first. As for the second, because death resulted from both wounds, so he is the one who destroyed half of it, and it is owned by another, so he guarantees half of its value while it is wounded with two wounds, because the first wound was not by his action, and the second wound he already guaranteed once, so he does not guarantee it again. As for the third, because with the first shot it became in a state that would have become permissible by optional slaughter had the second shot not occurred, so by the second shot he spoiled half of the meat for him, so he guarantees it, and he does not guarantee the other half because he guaranteed it once, and the guarantee of the meat is included in that.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from al-Ziyadat by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the liability of a second hunter when a hunted animal dies from wounds inflicted by two hunters, or when it is unknown which wound caused death. The ruling states that the second hunter must compensate three things: first, the decrease in value caused by his wound (daman al-nuqsan); second, half of the animal's value at the time it is wounded (i.e., after both wounds); third, half the value of the meat. The commentary then provides the reasoning: the first guarantee is because the second hunter directly caused a reduction in value of an animal owned by another. The second guarantee is because death resulted from both wounds, so the second hunter is considered to have destroyed half of the animal, and thus owes half its current wounded value. The first hunter's wound is not attributed to the second, and the second hunter's own wound is already compensated via the damage guarantee, so he does not guarantee it again. The third guarantee is because before the second shot, the animal could have been made halal by proper slaughter (dhabh), but the second shot spoiled half the meat, so he guarantees half the meat's value. The other half is already covered by the value guarantee. This example illustrates how Hanafi fiqh apportions liability when multiple parties contribute to a loss, based on the principle of direct causation and prevention of double compensation.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage caused to another's property.
جراحة (jarāḥa): A wound inflicted on the animal; used here to determine the extent of damage and causation of death.
ذكاة اختياري (dhakāt ikhtiyārī): Optional/voluntary slaughter (dhabh) that renders the animal halal; contrasted with forced slaughter due to injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Ziyādāt, what is the third guarantee the second hunter must pay?
A) The full value of the meat
B) Half the value of the meat
C) Quarter the value of the meat
D) No meat guarantee

Key Takeaway
The second hunter guarantees his own damage, half the animal's value from combined wounds, and half the meat value.

Three Compensations When Two Arrows Hit a Single Prey
Explains three compensations with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three compensations the second shooter must pay and explain the proof for each.

Core Idea
A second shooter who kills a wounded prey is liable for damage, half the value, and half the meat.

This ruling from al-Ziyadat (in Zahir al-Riwayah) addresses a nuanced scenario: a prey is wounded by a first arrow, then a second arrow kills it. The second shooter is liable for three distinct compensations: damage to the owner, half the value of the wounded animal, and half the meat value. The passage provides the reasoning for each, grounded in the principles of damage (itlaf) and ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the sequence
Recognize that there are two shooters: the first wounds the prey, the second wounds it again and death results from both wounds. The prey is owned by another.
The key is that the second shooter acts after the first, so the prey is already damaged.
2
Distinguish the three compensations
Note that the second shooter pays: (1) compensation for damage (nuqsan) caused by his wound, (2) half of the value of the wounded prey (because he destroyed half of it), and (3) half of the meat value (because he prevented lawful slaughter).
These are separate liabilities, not overlapping.
3
Analyze the proof for damage compensation
The second shooter caused additional damage to the prey by wounding it, so he is liable for that decrease in value.
The damage is the reduction in value from the first wound to the second wound.
4
Analyze the proof for half-value compensation
Since death resulted from both wounds, each shooter contributed to half the death. Therefore the second shooter is liable for half the value of the prey at the time of wounding, not the healthy value.
The value is assessed when the prey is already wounded (e.g., 12 rupees in the example).
5
Analyze the proof for meat compensation
After the first wound, the prey could have become halal through proper slaughter. The second shot prevented that, spoiling half the meat. The second shooter is liable for half the meat value because the other half was already lost due to the first wound.
This overlaps with the value compensation but is a separate aspect (halalness).

Source Text

Anyway, the first: because the second person wounded the prey that is owned by someone else and caused damage, so he is liable for the damage he caused. And anyway, the second: because death resulted from both wounds, so the second becomes the one who destroyed half of it while it was owned by someone else, so he is liable for half its value, while it is wounded with two wounds, because the first wound was not caused by him, and the second wound (although caused by him) he has already paid compensation for it once, so he will not be liable again. Explanation: The author stated the ruling of the mentioned case with reference to al-Ziyadat, which contains three things: compensation for damage, compensation for value, compensation for meat. Here the author gives the proof for each. The proof for compensation for damage: the second person wounded the prey owned by another, thereby damaging it, so compensation for damage is taken from him. The proof for compensation for value: when the death of the prey occurred from both wounds, the second person destroyed half of the prey owned by another, so half the value is obligatory upon him, but half of the value of the wounded prey, and the wounded prey is the one wounded with two wounds, which in the mentioned example was 12 rupees, half of which is 6 rupees. Question: Should compensation for the wounds also be obligatory on the second? Answer: The second had no involvement in the first wound, so no compensation is obligatory on him for it; as for his own wound, he has already paid compensation for damage earlier, so double compensation is not obligatory upon him. The proof for compensation for meat: As for the third: because by the first shot it became in a state that it would be permissible by optional slaughter if the second shot had not been made, so by the second shot he spoiled half the meat, so he is liable for it, and he is not liable for the other half because he was liable for it once, so the compensation for the meat is included. Translation: And anyway, the third: because the prey was in such a state after the first arrow that it would become halal by optional slaughter if the second arrow was not thrown, so the second person, by throwing the second arrow, spoiled half the meat, so he is liable for it, and he will not be liable for the other half because he was already liable once, so the compensation for meat is included. Explanation: This is the proof for compensation for half the meat value; the gist is that after the first wound, it had enough life that it could be slaughtered by optional slaughter if the second arrow had not been shot. After the first wound, optional slaughter was possible, then he shot the second arrow causing it to die, so now this prey is not halal.
