Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, an Urdu commentary on al-Hidayah. It deals with a scenario where two different shooters hit the same prey owned by someone else. The first shooter wounds it, the second shooter also wounds it, and death results from both wounds. The author states three types of compensation (ضمان) that the second shooter must pay: compensation for damage (ضمان نقصان) because the second shooter damaged the prey by wounding it; compensation for value (ضمان قیمت) because the second shooter destroyed half of the prey (since death was from both wounds, he is liable for half the value of the wounded prey, not the healthy prey); and compensation for meat (ضمان قیمت لحم) because after the first wound the animal could have been made halal by proper slaughter, but the second shot rendered it haram, so the second shooter spoils half the meat (since the other half was already ruined by the first wound and accounted for in the value compensation). The text then addresses a question: why isn't the second shooter also liable for the wound itself? Because the first wound is not his doing, and his own wound is already compensated for in the damage compensation. The proof for the meat compensation is based on the principle that the animal was in a state of potential halalness (by optional slaughter) before the second shot. Thus, the second shooter is liable for half the meat value. The three compensations are distinct and cover different aspects of the loss.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation for loss or damage; financial responsibility.
نقصان (nuqsan): Damage or decrease in value; here specifically the reduction in value caused by wounding.
قیمت (qimat): Value or price; here the market value of the wounded animal at the time of wounding.
لحم (lahm): Meat; here refers to the edible flesh of the prey.
ذکاة اختیاری (dhakah ikhtiyari): Optional or proper slaughter (Islamic method) as opposed to hunting by injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because he prevented the animal from becoming halal through proper slaughter
C) Because he consumed the meat
D) Because he owned the animal

Key Takeaway
When two shooters wound the same owned prey, the second shooter must pay three separate compensations: damage, half the value, and half the meat value.

Same Shooter Fires Twice: The Second Shot that Harams the Prey
Ruling with proof via qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the same hunter shoots the same animal twice and explain why the second shot makes the prey haram.

Core Idea
The second shot, not the first, determines the ruling: it transforms potential halal into haram.

In the previous lesson, we learned that if a different person shoots the animal a second time, it becomes haram. But what if the same hunter fires twice? The Hanafi school extends the same ruling: the second shot makes the prey unlawful. This passage uses a vivid analogy to cement the principle: after the first wound, the animal could have been made halal by proper slaughter, but the second shot kills it, so it becomes carrion (maytah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a hunter shooting the same prey twice. The ruling is that the prey is not halal (haram).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a qiyas (analogy): the author compares it to the case of a different shooter, and to an animal on a mountain top that is wounded and then shot again.
The analogy shows the common illa (effective cause).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
After the first wound, the animal could have been made halal through proper slaughter (dhabh). The second shot kills it without slaughter, so it becomes maytah (carrion) – that is why it is haram.
The second shot is muharram (that which makes unlawful).
4
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute)
Some other schools may permit the meat if the first wound was lethal and the second shot merely hastened death. The Hanafi school holds the second shot decisive.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Hanafi, the prey is haram; under Shafi'i view, it would be halal if the first wound was sufficient.
5
Apply the ruling
Whether the second shot is by the same hunter or another, the result is the same. The key is that the animal could have been slaughtered after the first wound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان كان رماه الأول ثانيا فالجواب في حكم الاباحة كالجواب فيما اذا كان الرامى غيره ويصير كما اذا رمی صیدا على قلة جبل فاتخنه ثم رماه ثانيا فانزله لا يحل لان الثاني محرم كذا هذا
ترجمہ اور اگر پہلے ہی شخص نے اس کو دوبارہ تیر مارا تو جواب اباحت کے حکم میں اس جواب کے مثل ہے جو اس صورت میں ہے کہ تیر مارنے والا اول کا غیر ہو اور یہ ایسا ہو جائے گا جبکہ اس نے کسی شکار کو پہاڑ کی چوٹی پر تیر مارا پس اس کو کاری ضرب لگائی پھر اس کو دوسرا تیر مارا پس اس کو نیچے گرا دیا تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ دوسرا تیر حرام کرنے والا ہے ایسے ہی یہ۔
تشریح پہلے زخم کے بعد ذکاۃ اختیاری ہو سکی تھی کہ پھر اس نے دوسرا تیر مار دیا جس سے وہ مر گیا تو اب یہ حلال نہیں رہا اس دلیل سے جو مذکور ہو چکی جیسے کسی نے پہاڑ کی چوٹی پر شکار کو تیر مار کر ضرب کاری لگائی جس سے وہ تیز امتناع سے خارج ہو گیا پھر اس نے اس کو دوسرا تیر مارا جس سے وہ نیچے گر گیا تو شکار حلال نہیں ہے کیونکہ حسب دلیل مذکور دوسرے تیر نے شکار کو حرام کر دیا ہے اسی طرح
yہاں کا حکم ہے۔
خلاصہ کلام دوسری مرتبہ وہ خود تیر مارے یا کوئی دوسرا شکار حلال نہ ہوگا۔
And if the first person shot it a second time, the answer regarding the ruling of permissibility is like the answer in the case where the shooter was someone else; and it becomes like when someone shot a prey on a mountain top and wounded it severely, then shot it a second time and brought it down, it is not halal because the second shot is what makes it unlawful; likewise this. Translation: And if the same person shoots it a second time, then the answer regarding the ruling of permissibility is like the answer in the case where the shooter is someone else; and it will be like when someone shot a prey on a mountain top, inflicted a severe wound, then shot it a second time and brought it down, it is not halal because the second shot is the one that makes it unlawful; similarly this. Explanation: After the first wound, optional slaughter was possible, then he shot a second arrow causing it to die, so now it is not halal, by the proof mentioned earlier; just as someone shot a prey on a mountain top with a severe wound that removed it from the state of resistance, then he shot it a second time and it fell, the prey is not halal because according to the mentioned proof, the second shot made the prey unlawful; similarly is the ruling here. Summary: Whether the same person shoots a second time or someone else, the prey will not be halal.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof passage that addresses a sub-case: if the same hunter shoots the same animal twice. The ruling is that the animal is not halal, just like when a different shooter fires the second shot. The text provides an analogy: if a hunter severely wounds an animal on a mountain top (so that it loses its ability to escape), then shoots it again causing it to fall, the second shot makes it haram. The same applies here: after the first wound, the animal could have been made halal by proper slaughter, but the second shot kills it, so it becomes carrion. The summary clarifies that whether the second shot is by the same person or another, the result is the same—the prey is not halal.

Key Terms
اباحت (ibahah): Permissibility; here specifically the ruling of halalness of hunting.
محرم (muharram): That which makes unlawful; the second shot is the cause of prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter shoots an animal with a non-lethal wound, then shoots it again and kills it, the animal is:
A) Halal because the hunter owns it
B) Halal because the first wound made it permissible
C) Not halal because the second shot makes it carrion
D) Makruh but permissible

Key Takeaway
The second shot, whether by the same hunter or another, makes the prey haram because it dies without proper slaughter.

The Hanafi Ruling on Hunting Non-Edible Animals
States the permissibility of hunting non-edible animals with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting non-edible animals and explain the proofs from the text.

Core Idea
Hunting non-edible animals is permissible, as the Quranic permission is general.

In this passage from al-Quduri’s matn, the Hanafi school establishes that hunting is not restricted to edible animals. While many assume hunting is solely for food, the text provides evidence from the Quran and practical benefits to permit the hunting of predators, pests, and other non-edible creatures. We will explore the proofs and the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The text states that hunting both edible and non-edible animals is permissible (jā'iz). This is the core hukm.
2
Examine the Primary Proof
The author cites 'the generality of what we recited', referring to Quranic verses that permit hunting without restricting to edible meat. Identify the Qur'anic basis.
Refer to Quran 5:96 and 5:1.
3
Analyze the Supporting Evidence
The text adds a poetic example and a rational argument: hunting non-edible animals yields benefits (skin, hair, pest control) which are legislated.
4
Understand the Hanafi Legal Reasoning
The permission is not limited to edible meat because the general texts are unrestricted ('āmm). The benefit (manfa'ah) is a valid secondary proof.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Some schools may restrict hunting to edible animals only; this passage clarifies the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار ماکول اللحم اور غیر ماکول دونوں جانوروں کا جائز ہے
قال ويجوز اصطياد ما يوكل لحمه من الحيوان وما لا يوكل لا طلاق ما تلونا والصيد لا يختص بماكول اللحم قال قائلهم شعر صید الملوک از نب وثعالب واذا ركبت فضيدى الابطال ولان صيده سبب للانتفاع بجلده او شعره او ریشه اولا ستدفاع شره وكل ذلك مشروع والله اعلم بالصواب
Hunting both edible and non-edible animals is permissible. One of their poets said: The hunting of kings are rabbits and foxes, and when I ride, my hunting are brave champions. Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the text of al-Quduri (matn) and its commentary. It states that hunting is permissible for both edible and non-edible animals. The proof is the general verses of the Quran (like 'حل لكم صيد البحر وطعامه' and 'أحل لكم صيد البر') which do not restrict to edible animals. Additionally, hunting non-edible animals is beneficial: their skin can be used, their hair/wool, feathers, or to ward off harm (like pests). A poet's verse is cited as an example that kings hunt rabbits and foxes (non-edible), showing it was common. Therefore, the ruling is that hunting non-edible animals is permissible and legislated.

Key Terms
ماکول اللحم (ma'kul al-lahm): Animals whose meat is considered edible and permissible to eat.
غیر ماکول اللحم (ghayr ma'kul al-lahm): Animals whose meat is not eaten (e.g., predators, pests).
اصطياد (ishtiyad): Hunting or capturing animals, usually by means of weapons or traps.
استدفاع الشر (istidfa' al-sharr): Repelling harm (as a justification for hunting).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Second Shot Kills Wounded Animal: Haram
Ruling with proof by analogy (qiyas jaliy)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a second shot killing a wounded animal renders the meat haram, and explain the qiyas used to derive this ruling.

Core Idea
A second shot that kills a wounded animal prevents proper slaughter, making the meat haram.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in hunting law: when a hunter wounds an animal, then a second shot kills it before it can be properly slaughtered. The Hanafi school rules that the meat becomes haram, regardless of who fires the second shot. The proof is established through analogy (qiyas) with a case where a severely wounded animal on a mountain peak is killed by a second shot. Understanding this ruling is essential for applying Islamic hunting ethics and understanding the role of intention and causality in legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a second shot that kills an already wounded animal. The Hanafi ruling is that the meat becomes haram (unlawful).
The first wound alone did not kill; the second shot is the direct cause of death.
2
Analyze the proof: qiyas
The proof is an analogy to a case where a hunter shoots an animal on a mountain peak, severely wounding it, then shoots a second time, killing it. In that case, the meat is haram because the second shot is the cause of death.
The analogy is explicit in the text: 'it becomes like when he shot game on a mountain peak...'
3
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that the second shot is the direct cause of death (muharram) and prevents proper slaughter (dhabh) that was possible after the first wound.
The first wound did not make the animal halal; it only made dhabh possible. The second shot eliminates that possibility.
4
Note the ruling applies whether same or different shooter
The text explicitly states that the ruling is the same whether the second shot is by the same hunter or a different one.
The second shooter is also liable for compensation (dhaman) for the value of the meat.
5
Summarize the practical consequence
The meat is haram and cannot be consumed. The second shooter must compensate the owner for the meat value.
This ruling emphasizes the importance of proper slaughter before death.

Source Text

خلاصہ کلام دوسری مرتبہ وہ خود تیر مارے یا کوئی دوسرا شکار حلال نہ ہوگا۔
Then the other shot an arrow making its meat haram, so he will be guarantor for that, and the value of the other half of the meat will not be obligatory on him because he has already paid the guarantee for half of that game, which includes the guarantee of the meat. Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof by analogy (qiyas). It addresses the scenario where a hunter wounds an animal (making it possible to render halal by proper slaughter), but then a second shot kills it. The ruling: the animal becomes haram, whether the second shot is by the same or a different hunter. The text provides an analogy: if an animal on a mountain peak is severely wounded (such that it cannot escape) and then a second shot kills it, it is haram because the second shot is the direct cause of death (muharram). Similarly, in the case at hand, the first wound did not kill; the second shot did, and since the animal was not properly slaughtered (dhabh), it remains haram. The proof is that the second shot is analogous to a prohibited act that invalidates potential halal status. The commentary follows the author's order: first states the ruling for a different hunter, then for the same hunter, then provides the analogy, and concludes with a summary.

ذکاۃ (dhabh): Ritual slaughter that makes meat halal
ضامن (dhamin): Guarantor responsible for compensation

Key Takeaway
A second shot that kills a wounded animal before proper slaughter renders the meat haram, whether fired by the same or a different hunter, due to the analogy with the mountain peak case.

Permissibility of Hunting Non-Edible Animals
Ruling with proof from Quran and linguistic usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting non-edible animals and explain the Quranic and linguistic evidence.

Core Idea
Hunting non-edible animals is mubah because the Quranic command is general and 'sayd' linguistically includes more than food.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling that hunting is not restricted to edible animals. The text from Al-Hidayah provides a clear proof from the Quran and linguistic usage. By understanding the evidence, you will see how the Hanafi school derives broad permissibility for hunting when there is benefit (skin, hair, feathers) or harm prevention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text states that hunting (sayd) of both edible and non-edible animals is permissible (ja'iz). Note: the ruling is mubah (permissible), not wajib or haram.
The ruling applies to any animal that can be hunted; focus on the scope.
2
Locate the primary evidence (Quran)
The author cites the Quranic verse: 'When you are lawful, then hunt' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:2). This is the main proof. The command is general ('amm) and not restricted to edible animals.
The verse is absolute (mutlaq); therefore, it covers all types of hunting.
3
Analyze the linguistic evidence
The text invokes a pre-Islamic poet who used 'sayd' for brave men, proving the word is not exclusive to edible meat. This linguistic usage reinforces the general applicability of the ruling.
Even though human meat is haram, the term 'sayd' can be applied to non-edible things.
4
Note the rational benefits (hikmah)
The text mentions that hunting non-edible animals allows using their skin, hair, feathers, or repelling their harm. These are legitimate purposes in Shariah, supporting the permissibility.
This is an extra argument (talil) that strengthens the ruling.
5
Consider the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Some other schools may restrict hunting to edible animals. The Hanafi school follows the general proof. The practical difference (thamarah) is that Hanafis allow hunting foxes, birds of prey, etc. for benefit.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate (mukhtalaf fih).

Source Text

All these purposes are recognized as legitimate in Shariah. The text follows the order: first states the ruling, then provides the Quranic evidence, then the linguistic evidence (poetry), and finally the rational benefits. The commentary affirms that the absolute nature of the verse suffices as proof.

Hunting Permissible for Edible and Non-Edible Animals
Proves hunting is permissible for both edible and non-edible animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting non-edible animals and explain the proof from the Quranic verse and Arabic linguistic usage.

Core Idea
Hunting is permissible for all animals because the Quranic command is absolute and the word 'sayd' is not restricted to edible meat.

This lesson addresses a common question: Is it permissible to hunt animals that are not halal to eat, such as predators or pests? The Hanafi school holds a clear position based on the general evidence of the Quran and the Arabic language. We will explore the proof and contrast it with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine the legal ruling (hukm) for hunting both edible and non-edible animals according to the Hanafi school.
The text states both types are permissible (ja'iz).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary evidence cited from the Quran (Quran 5:2) and note its textual form.
The verse is an absolute command: 'When you are permitted, then hunt.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the verse proves the ruling. According to Hanafi usul, an absolute command (mutlaq) remains general unless restricted.
No restriction exists, so it applies to all animals.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the opposing view (Shafi'i) and their basis for restricting hunting to edible animals.
Shafi'is argue that the primary purpose of hunting is food, so non-edible animals are excluded.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal using linguistic evidence: the word 'sayd' is applied in Arabic to non-edible creatures, as shown in poetry.
This supports the general permissibility.

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah is a ruling statement that clarifies the permissibility of hunting both edible (ma'kul al-lahm) and non-edible (ghayr ma'kul al-lahm) animals. The author begins by stating the ruling: both types are permissible (ja'iz). The proof is the Quranic verse 'And when you are permitted [to hunt], then hunt' (Quran 5:2), which is absolute (mutlaq) and does not specify edible meat. Additionally, a linguistic argument is presented: the word 'sayd' (hunting) is used in Arabic poetry for brave men, even though human meat is never permissible. This shows that 'sayd' is not restricted to edible creatures. Therefore, the general permissibility includes all types of animals. The commentator ends by noting that the rest of the explanation is straightforward, implying that further details about conditions of hunting are covered elsewhere.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Hunting or the hunted animal; in this passage, it refers to the act of hunting as well as the quarry.
ماكول اللحم (ma'kul al-lahm): Animals whose flesh is permissible to eat according to Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, why is hunting non-edible animals permissible?
A) Because the Quran specifically mentions non-edible animals.
B) Because the word 'sayd' in the Quran and Arabic is general, not restricted to edible meat.
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly permitted it.
D) Because non-edible animals are harmful and must be eliminated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that hunting all animals is permissible because the primary textual evidence is general and unrestricted.

Linguistic and Legal Definition of Rahn
Defining rahn linguistically and legally with proof from Quran and poetry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic meaning of rahn (permanent detention) and identify the Quranic and poetic proof for this meaning, and explain how the legal definition builds on the linguistic root.

Core Idea
Rahn linguistically means permanent detention, proven by Quran and poetry, forming the basis of the mortgagee's right to detain the pledge.

This lesson explores the foundational definition of rahn (pledge) in Hanafi fiqh. The author establishes that the core meaning is permanent detention (habs da'im), supported by a Quranic verse and a line of pre-Islamic poetry. This linguistic meaning directly informs the legal ruling: the mortgagee may possess and detain the pledged property until the debt is paid, but ownership does not transfer.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core claim
Identify the author's main assertion about the meaning of rahn. Look for the phrase 'habs da'im' (permanent detention).
The author states that rahn linguistically means permanent detention.
2
Locate the primary proof (Quran)
Find the Quranic verse cited and understand how it supports the claim. The verse 'kullu nafsin bima kasabat rahinah' (74:38) uses the word rahinah in the sense of being detained for one's deeds.
The verse is used to show that rahn inherently implies being held in pledge, i.e., detained.
3
Locate the secondary proof (poetry)
Identify the poetic line and its translation. The poet says his heart is a rahn with his beloved, permanently detained with no release, reinforcing the idea of continuous restraint.
Poetry is used as linguistic evidence, common in Hanafi usul for clarifying literal meanings.
4
Relate linguistic meaning to legal concept
Note the author's conclusion: because rahn linguistically means permanent detention, this meaning is incorporated into the legal definition, though shar'i realities may not completely align with linguistic ones.
The legal definition builds on the linguistic root, but not identically; the mortgagee has a possessory right, not ownership.
5
Confirm the key terms
Define the terms 'rahn', 'habs da'im', and 'yad al-istifa' as used in the passage. Understand their relationship.
Yad al-istifa' is the hand of recovery—the mortgagee's right to possess and detain for debt recovery.

With such a pledge for which there is no release on the day of departure, it became a detained thing, and Islamic rulings incline towards informing words accordingly. Commentary: Here the author is laying a foundation for his intended purpose and will later say that from this it is established that the pledge is bound. He says that the mortgagee has the right to recover his debt through the pledge, which the author expressed as 'istifa' (recovery). Then regarding this 'yad al-istifa' (hand of recovery), he said that it is possession and ownership, meaning the mortgagee has the right to take possession of the pledge and keep it detained. Now the author presents evidence for this detention: the linguistic meaning of rahn is permanent detention, proven by the Quranic verse and poetry. The verse: 'Every soul is held in pledge for what it earns' (Quran 74:38) means every person will be detained for their deeds. So rahn means permanent detention. The poet says to his beloved: 'On the day of farewell, I parted from you while my heart is with you as a pledge, detained with permanent detention that cannot be released' meaning it has become your property. Here too rahn is in the sense of permanent detention. When linguistically rahn means permanent detention, then this meaning will also be considered in Islamic law. In other words, shar'i realities do not completely agree with linguistic realities.

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of rahn (pledge/pawn) linguistically and legally. The author begins by establishing that rahn involves detention (habs). The mortgagee (murtahin) has the right to possess and detain the pledged property as security for the debt. The proof for this detention is the linguistic meaning of rahn: permanent detention (habs da'im). The author cites the Quranic verse 'kullu nafsin bima kasabat rahinah' (Every soul is held in pledge for its deeds) to show that rahn linguistically means detention. He also cites a line of poetry where a lover says his heart is a rahn with his beloved, meaning it is permanently detained. Although the passage notes that shar'i realities (haqa'iq shar'iyyah) may differ from linguistic realities (haqa'iq lughawiyyah), the core meaning of detention is retained in the legal concept of rahn. This is a common Hanafi approach: the legal definition builds on the linguistic root without being identical.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Linguistically permanent detention (habs da'im); legally a contract where property is detained as security for a debt.
حبس دائم (habs da'im): Permanent detention, the core linguistic meaning of rahn, indicating continuous restraint until the debt is paid.
ید الاستیفاء (yad al-istifa'): The hand/right of recovery; the mortgagee's right to recover the debt through the pledged property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi legal definition of rahn retains the core linguistic meaning of permanent detention, justifying the mortgagee's right to hold the pledge until the debt is paid, without transferring ownership.

Rahn as a Security for Debt Recovery
Explaining that rahn is a security that ensures debt recovery through possession and detention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school defines rahn as a wathiqah that ensures debt recovery through possession (milk al-yad) and detention (habs), and identify the two functions that motivate repayment.

Core Idea
Rahn secures debt recovery by giving the creditor possession, preventing denial and pressuring the debtor to repay.

This lesson explores the second purpose of rahn (pledge) as outlined in the Hanafi tradition. The author explains that rahn is not merely a physical transfer but a security mechanism that psychologically compels the debtor to repay. By understanding the two functions of possession—preventing denial and restricting benefit—you will grasp the legal wisdom behind this contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose
Recognize that rahn is a wathiqah (security) for debt recovery. It is designed to lead to payment.
2
Understand the mechanism
The security is achieved through milk al-yad (ownership of possession) and habs (detention). These give the creditor control over the pledged item.
Milk al-yad is distinct from ownership; the creditor only holds, does not own.
3
Analyze the first function
Possession prevents the debtor from denying the debt and the creditor from denying the pledge, creating mutual security from denial (al-amn min al-juhud).
4
Analyze the second function
Detention makes the debtor unable to benefit from the asset, creating psychological pressure (need or distress) to repay quickly.
This motivation is a key aspect of rahn's effectiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second link of the preliminary to the main purpose: 'Because the pledge is a security for the side of recovery, and that is that it leads to it, and that is established by ownership of possession and detention so that security from denial occurs for fear of the mortgagee's denial of the pledge, and so that the pledgor becomes unable to benefit from it, and thus he hastens to pay the debt due to his need or his distress.' Translation: And because the pledge is a strength for the side of recovery, and strength is that it leads to recovery, and this (the security leading to recovery) will be from ownership of possession and detention so that security from denial occurs for fear of the mortgagee's denial of the pledge, and so that the pledgor becomes unable to benefit from the pledged item, so the pledgor hastens to pay the debt due to his need or his mental distress. Commentary: This is the second link of the preliminary to the main purpose. Its summary is that the pledge is a means to solidify recovery so that through this solidity access to recovery can be achieved. And this solidity's leading to recovery is established by ownership of hand and ownership, meaning when the mortgagee takes possession of the pledge, it becomes a means for him to recover the debt because now the pledgor cannot deny the debt and he will fear that the mortgagee might deny the pledge. Also, the mortgagee's possession is a means because the pledgor's benefit from the pledge is cut off while he needs it, or he has distress that his valuable item is with someone as a pledge, so he will try to repay the debt quickly. This was the preliminary; now the author states the main purpose of the Book of Pledge.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage explains the second purpose of rahn: it acts as a security (wathiqah) that ensures debt recovery. The author states that rahn is a wathiqah (security) for the side of istifa' (recovery) because it leads to recovery. This is achieved through the mortgagee's ownership of possession (milk al-yad) and detention (habs). Possession serves two functions: first, it prevents the pledgor from denying the debt (since the pledge is in the mortgagee's hands), and second, it prevents the mortgagee from denying the pledge (since he has possession). Additionally, detention makes the pledgor unable to benefit from the pledged item, which motivates him to repay the debt quickly, either because he needs the item or because he is distressed that his valuable property is held. This is presented as a preliminary (tamhid) to the main discussion of the Book of Pledge.

Key Terms
وثیقہ (wathiqah): Security or bond; rahn is described as a wathiqah for debt recovery, meaning it strengthens the creditor's claim.
ملک الید (milk al-yad): Ownership of possession; the mortgagee's right to physically hold the pledged item without owning it.
حبس (habs): Detention; the mortgagee's right to keep the pledged item in his custody until the debt is paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what two purposes does the mortgagee's possession (milk al-yad and habs) serve?
A) To allow the mortgagee to use the pledged item and to generate profit
B) To prevent denial by both parties and to motivate the pledgor to repay
C) To transfer ownership to the mortgagee and to end the contract
D) To ensure the pledged item is safe and to allow the mortgagee to sell it

Key Takeaway
Rahn is a security contract that uses possession and detention to prevent denial and motivate repayment, not to grant ownership or usage.

Ruling on Double Recovery After Pledge Destruction
Ruling that double recovery after pledge destruction is riba, with proof and rebuttal of an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that double recovery after pledge destruction is haram and distinguish between scenarios where the pledge exists versus when it is destroyed.

Core Idea
Double recovery after pledge destruction is riba; the debt is considered recovered in one sense and confirmed by destruction.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a subtle scenario: if the pledged property is destroyed, the mortgagee's recovery of the debt is established 'min wajh' (in one sense) and strengthened by the destruction. Therefore, attempting to recover the debt again from the pledgor would constitute riba. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including a key distinction from when the pledge still exists, and a rational proof using an analogy with counterfeit coinage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the mortgagee recovering the debt a second time after the pledge is destroyed. The ruling: haram because it leads to riba.
This is the core ruling of the passage.
2
Understand the concept of 'min wajh' recovery
The first recovery is considered partial (min wajh) but is confirmed (taqarrara) by destruction. This means the debt is satisfied in one aspect, making a second recovery an impermissible increase.
Hanafi uses 'min wajh' to indicate partial satisfaction of debt.
3
Distinguish the case when the pledge exists
If the pledge is still intact, returning it to the pledgor nullifies the first recovery, preventing double recovery and thus no riba.
This distinction shows the ruling is specific to destruction.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objection is raised: could the mortgagee recover the remaining 'raqabah' (ownership) after destruction without riba? The response: without possession, recovery of the remainder is inconceivable, so any attempt leads to riba.
The objection explores the difference between milk al-yad and milk al-raqabah.
5
Apply the analogy with counterfeit coins
The author provides an analogy: if a debtor pays with counterfeit coins and the creditor spends them, the right to good coins is forfeited because recovery would involve riba. This parallels the pledge destruction case.
The analogy reinforces that once recovery is confirmed, a second claim is prohibited.

Source Text

A mas'ala based on the preliminaries: 'And when it is so, recovery is established in one respect, and it is confirmed by destruction. So if he recovers it a second time, it leads to riba, unlike the case when the pledge exists, because this recovery is nullified by returning it to the pledgor, so it does not recur.' Translation: And when the matter is such, recovery from one aspect is established, and this recovery is strengthened by destruction. So if he recovers it again, it leads to riba, unlike the case when the pledge exists, because this recovery will be broken by returning it to the pledgor, so recovery will not be repeated. Commentary: From all these preliminaries, it became clear that the mortgagee has obtained his right in one sense, and if the pledged item is destroyed, this recovery is further strengthened. Therefore, if the mortgagee recovers his debt again, it would involve riba. Question: If the pledge exists and the debt is recovered from the pledgor, would double recovery also necessitate riba here? No, because when the pledge exists, it will be returned to the pledgor, so no repetition of recovery occurs. Response: 'And there is no way to recover the remainder without possession because it is inconceivable.' Commentary: This response is to a presumed objection. The objection is: You have declared recovery of the debt after destruction of the pledge as riba, but we can present a case where the debt is recovered twice without riba: the mortgagee had only milk al-yad (possession) not milk al-raqabah (ownership), and when the pledge was destroyed, the milk al-yad was recovered, leaving the milk al-raqabah to be recovered from the pledgor. So recovering only the raqabah would not be riba. The response: Without milk al-yad, repetition of recovery in the remaining right would occur, leading to riba. An example: A person owes another one thousand good dirhams. The debtor lost the dirhams, and the creditor spent them in his need, then learned they were counterfeit. Then the right to good dirhams becomes void because there is no way to recover good dirhams without involving riba. Similarly in the mentioned case.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial ruling: if the pledged property is destroyed, the debt is considered recovered in one sense (min wajh), and this recovery is confirmed (taqarrara) by destruction. Therefore, if the mortgagee (murtahin) tries to recover the debt again from the pledgor (rahin), it would constitute riba (usury), because the debt is already considered satisfied. This is different from when the pledge still exists, because if the mortgagee recovers the debt while the pledge is intact, he must return the pledge, and the recovery is nullified (yantaqid) in that sense, so no double recovery occurs. The passage then addresses an objection: what about the case where the mortgagee only had possession rights (milk al-yad) and not ownership rights (milk al-raqabah)? After destruction, the possession right is considered recovered, but the ownership right remains outstanding. Could the mortgagee recover the ownership right without riba? The response is no, because without possession, recovering the remnant (baqi) is inconceivable; any attempt to do so would necessarily involve repetition of recovery (takrar al-istifa'), which leads to riba. An analogy is given: if a debtor pays a debt with counterfeit coins and the creditor spends them, then the right to good coins is forfeited because recovery is impossible without riba.

Key Terms
استيفاء (istifa'): Recovery or collection of the debt; here it refers to the mortgagee's satisfaction of the debt through the pledge.
من وجہ (min wajh): In one sense or aspect; partial recovery. Here it means the debt is considered recovered in one respect (through possession) but not fully.
ربوا (riba): Usury or interest; any increase in a loan without corresponding compensation. Here, double recovery is considered riba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that recovering the debt a second time after the pledge is destroyed leads to riba?
A) Because the pledge is still owned by the pledgor
B) Because the first recovery is considered established (taqarrara) by destruction, making a second recovery a forbidden increase
C) Because the mortgagee no longer has possession
D) Because the debtor has already paid the debt in full

Key Takeaway
Double recovery after pledge destruction is riba because the destruction confirms the first recovery, making a second claim an impermissible increase.

Custom Distinguishes Use from Protection in Pledge
States ruling for two vs three swords based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that custom ('urf) distinguishes use from protection in pledge liability, and derive the ruling for any number of pledged swords based on prevailing custom.

Core Idea
Custom determines whether hanging a pledged sword is use or protection.

In Hanafi law, a pledgee who uses the pledged item without permission becomes liable for it. However, not every action counts as 'use'—some actions are merely protective. This lesson examines a ruling from Al-Hidayah that distinguishes between hanging two swords (use, liable) and three swords (protection, not liable) based on the custom of warriors. Understanding this distinction helps us apply the same reasoning to other pledged items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State clearly: For two swords, the pledgee is liable (damin). For three swords, he is not liable.
Note that the number of swords changes the ruling, not the act itself.
2
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof is custom ('urf): it is customary among brave men to wear two swords in battle, but not three.
Custom is a recognized secondary source in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Distinguish use vs. protection
If the action follows customary practice, it is considered use (isti'mal). If it deviates from custom, it is protection (hifz).
Liability attaches only to use, not protection.
4
Apply the principle
For any number of swords, check the prevailing custom. If custom supports that number, it is use; otherwise, it is protection.
This principle can be extended to other pledged items by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رهنه سيفين اوثلثة فتقلدها لم يضمن فى الثلثة وضمن فى السيفين لان العادة جرت بين الشجعان بتقلد سيفين في الحرب ولم تجر بتقلد الثالثة
ترجمہ اور اگر مرتہن کے پاس دو یا تین تلوار رکھیں پس مرتہن نے ان تلواروں کو لٹکا یا تو ضامن نہ ہوگا تین میں اور دو تلوار میں ضامن ہوگا اس لئے کہ عادت جاری ہے بہادروں کے درمیان جنگ میں دو تلواروں کے لٹکانے کی اتحادت جاری نہیں ہے تین تلوار لٹکانے کی ۔ تشریح گلے میں دو تلوار ڈال کر چلنے کی عادت ہے اور تین کی عادت نہیں ہے لہذا اگر مرتہن نے تین تلوار گلے میں ڈالیں تو اسکو استعمال نہیں کہا جائیگا بلکہ یہ حفاظت ہے اور اگر دولٹکائی تو یہ استعمال ہے اور اب ضامن ہوگا اور تین میں ضامن نہ ہوگا۔
And if he pledges two or three swords, and he hangs them, he is not liable for the three, and he is liable for the two, because custom among the brave has spread for hanging two swords in war, and it has not spread for hanging the third. / Translation: If the pledgee has two or three swords placed with him, and the pledgee hangs them, then he will not be liable for the three, and he will be liable for the two swords, because custom is current among the brave for hanging two swords in war, and hanging three swords is not current. / Explanation: The custom of walking with two swords hung around the neck exists, and for three it does not. Therefore, if the pledgee hangs three swords around his neck, this will not be called use; rather it is protection. And if he hangs two, this is use, and thus he will be liable, and for three he will not be liable.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof based on custom. The ruling distinguishes between two and three pledged swords hung around the neck. The key principle is that if the act is considered 'use' (isti'mal) of the pledged item, the pledgee becomes liable (damin) for it. If it is considered 'protection' (hifaz), he is not liable. Custom ('urf) is the criterion: among warriors, it is customary to wear two swords in battle, so hanging two is considered use. Wearing three is not customary, so it is regarded as protection. The commentary emphasizes that the liability depends on whether the action is a customary use. This ruling is from the Hanafi school and reflects reliance on 'urf as a secondary source of law.

Key Terms
تقلد (taqlid): To hang something around the neck, here specifically swords.
شجعان (shuja'an): Brave men or warriors, the people whose custom is considered.
عادت ('adat): Custom, habitual practice that serves as a legal indicator of use or protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a pledgee liable for hanging two pledged swords but not three?
A) Because two swords are worth less than three.
B) Because custom considers hanging two swords as use, but three as protection.
C) Because the pledgee only intended to protect three swords.
D) Because the pledgor permitted two but not three.

Key Takeaway
Liability for using a pledged item depends on custom: customary use triggers liability, non-customary protection does not.

Liability for Wearing a Pledged Ring Over Another Ring
Custom-based ruling for pledged ring use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the custom-based criterion to determine whether a pledgee's action constitutes use or protection.

Core Idea
Custom determines whether wearing a pledged ring is use (liable) or protection (not liable).

This lesson expands on the principle that unauthorised use of pledged property triggers liability. The specific case of wearing a pledged ring over another ring is examined. The Hanafi jurists rule that the decisive factor is not the ring's value but the pledgee's customary practice: if he normally wears two rings for adornment, the extra ring is 'use' and he is liable; otherwise, it is 'protection' and he is not liable. This shows how custom (urf) shapes legal rulings in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal significance
The pledgee places a second ring (possibly the pledged ring) over another ring on the same finger. We must determine if this counts as 'use' (isti'mal) of the pledged item or mere 'protection' (hifz).
The act itself is not inherently prohibited; the ruling hinges on its classification.
2
Recognise the role of custom (urf)
The text states that the criterion is whether the pledgee is 'among those who adorn themselves with two rings'. Custom indicates the purpose and typical use. If wearing two rings is customary adornment, the action is use; otherwise, it is protective.
Custom is determined by the social practice of the pledgee's community and his own habits.
3
Apply the conditional ruling
If the pledgee normally wears two rings for adornment, he is liable (damin) for the pledged ring because he has used it without permission. If he does not, the extra ring is considered a protective measure, and he is not liable.
Liability means the pledgee must compensate the pledgor if the pledged ring is damaged or lost, as use invalidates the solemnity of the pledge.
4
Extract the underlying principle (talil)
The ruling is based on the broader rule that any customary use of pledged property without consent makes the pledgee a usurper (ghasib) and thus liable. The illa (effective cause) is the customary nature of the act as adornment.
This is a case of qiyas talili, where the cause is derived from the text's indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان لبس خاتما فوق خاتم ان كان هو ممن يتجمل بلبس خاتمين ضمن وان كان لا يتجمل بذلك فهو حافظ فلا يضمن
ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے انگوٹھی پر انگوٹھی پہنی اور اگر وہ ان لوگوں میں ہے ہو جو دو انگوٹھی پہن کر زینت اختیار کرتا ہے تو ضامن ہوگا اور اگر وہ اس سے زینت حاصل نہ کرتا ہو تو وہ حفاظت کرنے والا ہے پس ضامن نہ ہوگا۔
تشریح مرتہن نے رہن کی انگوٹھی ایک ہی انگلی میں دو پہن لی تو اب کیا حکم ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ اس کا مدار عادت پر ہے یعنی اگروہ ایک انگلی میں ڈبل ڈبل انگوٹھی پہننے کا عادی ہے تو اس کو استعمال کہا جائے گا اور ضمان واجب ہوگا اور اگر وہ اس طریقہ کا عادی نہیں ہے تو اس کو از قبیل حفاظت شمار کیا جائے گا اور ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔
And if he wears a ring over a ring, then if he is among those who adorn themselves by wearing two rings, he is liable; and if he does not adorn himself with that, then he is a protector, so he is not liable. / Translation: And if he wears a ring over another ring, and if he is among those who adorn themselves by wearing two rings, then he is liable; and if he does not adorn himself with that, then he is a protector, so he is not liable. / Explanation: The pledgee wore two rings on one finger that were pledged; what is the ruling? He said that its criterion is custom: if he is accustomed to wearing double rings on one finger, then it will be called use, and guarantee (daman) is obligatory. And if he is not accustomed to this method, then it will be considered from the category of protection, and guarantee is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, again based on custom. The issue is when a pledgee wears two rings on one finger, one of which is pledged. The ruling depends on whether wearing two rings is customary for that person as adornment. If it is, the action is considered 'use' (isti'mal), and the pledgee is liable (damin) for the pledged ring. If he does not normally wear two rings, then placing the second ring is deemed 'protection' (hifaz), and he is not liable. The commentary clarifies that the criterion is the custom of the individual and the society. This ruling extends the principle from the previous case to another item, showing the general rule that liability depends on whether the pledgee's action is a customary use of the pledged property.

Key Terms
خاتم (khatam): Ring, specifically a signet or finger ring.
يتجمل (yatajammalu): To adorn oneself, indicating the purpose of the action.
ضامن (damin): Liable, responsible for compensation.
حافظ (hafiz): Protector, one who keeps something safe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever the ring is valuable.
B) Only if he normally wears two rings as adornment.
C) Only if the pledgor allows it.
D) Always, because wearing a ring is considered use.

Key Takeaway
Liability for using pledged property hinges on customary practice, not mere physical contact.

Guarding and Maintenance Expenses in Rahn (Mortgage)
General principle with proof and application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who bears the cost of guarding and maintenance of pledged property and apply the general principle of 'hifz vs islah' to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Guarding expenses are on pledgee; maintenance on pledgor—ownership determines liability.

When property is pledged, who pays for its care? This lesson presents the Hanafi rule: expenses for guarding (hifz) fall on the pledgee, while expenses for improvement and maintenance (islah wa ibqa') fall on the pledgor. The reasoning is drawn from ownership and analogy to deposits. We will explore the general principle, its proof, and a specific application regarding a shepherd's wages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that guarding expenses (house rent, guard's wage) are on the pledgee; maintenance expenses (food, shepherd, clothing) are on the pledgor. First, identify who is the pledgee (murtahin) and pledgor (rahin) and what types of expenses are listed.
2
State the general principle
The passage gives a general rule: whatever is needed for the benefit (mislah) and preservation (ibqa') of the pledged property is on the owner (rahin). Guarding is distinct because it benefits the pledgee's security.
The principle is derived form the fact that the property remains in the pledgor's ownership.
3
Follow the proof: analogy to deposit (wadi'ah)
The text draws an analogy from the law of deposit. Just as in a deposit, the owner bears maintenance costs, so too in a mortgage. Identify the analogy: both cases involve property held by another (depositary/pledgee) but ownership remains with the owner.
The qiyas is jaliy (clear analogy) because the 'illa (effective cause) is ownership.
4
Examine the special case of the shepherd
The passage anticipates an objection: the shepherd both grazes (maintenance) and protects (guarding). The Hanafi response: the primary purpose is grazing, evidenced by the hadd penalty not being applied for theft from a pasture. This shows that the shepherd's role is primarily maintenance.
The hadd penalty is cut off only from a guarded (hirz) place; a pasture is not a hirz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واجرة البيت الذى يحفظ فيه الرهن على المرتهن وكذلك اجرة الحافظ واجرة الراعى ونفقة الرهن على الراهن والاصل ان ما يحتاج اليه لمصلحة الرهن وتبقيته فهو على الراهن سواء كان في الرهن فضل اولم يكن لان العين باق على ملكه وكذلك منافعه مملوكة له فيكون صلاحه وتبقيته عليه لما انه مؤنة ملکه کما في الوديعة وذلك مثل النفقة فى ماكله ومشربه واجرة الراعي في معناه لانه علف الحيوان
ترجمہ اور اس گھر کی اجرت جس میں مرہون کی حفاظت کی جائے گی مرتہن کے ذمہ ہے اور ایسے ہی نگرانی کرنے والے کی اجرت اور چرانے والے کی اجرت اور مرہون کا نفقہ را بہن کے ذمہ ہے اور قاعدہ کلیہ یہ ہے کہ جس چیز کی حاجت پیش آئے مرہون کی مصلحت کے لئے اور اس کو باقی رکھنے کیلئے تو وہ راہن کے ذمہ ہے خواہ رہن دین پر زیادتی ہو یا نہ ہو اس لئے کہ عین اس کی ملک پر باقی ہے اور ایسے ہی اس کے منافع راہن کے مملوک ہیں تو اس کی اصلاح اور اس کو باقی رکھنا راہن کے ذمہ ہوگا کیونکہ وہ اس کی ملک کی مؤنت ہے جیسا کہ ودیعت میں اور یہ جیسے اس کے کھانے اور پینے کا نفقہ ہے اور چرانے والے کی اجرت اسی کے درجہ میں ہے
تشریح ماقبل میں یہ بات گذر چکی ہے کہ مرہون مرتہن کے پاس محبوس رہے گا اب اس پر یہ سوال پیدا ہوتا ہے کہ اس کا نفقہ کس پر واجب ہوگا نیز جس مکان میں اس کی حفاظت کی جائے اس کا کرایہ کون دے گا نیز اس کی نگرانی کے لئے جس اجیر کو چھوڑا جائے تو اس کی مزدوری کون دیگا ؟
تو اس سلسلہ میں ایک قاعدہ کلیہ بیان فرمایا گیا ہے کہ جو چیز حفاظت کے باب سے ہوگی وہ تو مرتہن کے ذمہ ہے اور جو کام اصلاح و ابقاء کے باب سے ہو گا وہ راہن کے ذمہ ہے بس قاعدہ کلیہ یہی ہے اور بہر صورت یہی حکم ہے رہن کی قیمت قرض سے زیادہ ہو تب بھی اور برابر اور کم ہوتب بھی لہذا جس کمرہ میں اس کو رکھا جائیگا یہ باب حفاظت سے ہے اور اسی طرح نگراں کا مقرر کرنا بھی حفاظت کے باب سے ہے لہذا مکان کا کرایہ اورنگراں کی مزدوری مرتہن پر واجب ہوگی ۔
اور چرانے والے کی اجرت اور مرہون کا نفقہ اور کپڑا اسب راہن کے ذمہ ہوگا اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ مرہمون کا عین راہن کی ملکیت ہے اور منافع مرہون بھی راہن کی ملکیت ہے اور ملکیت کے لئے جو چیز اصلاح و ابقاء کے واسطے ہوتی ہے وہ مالک کے ذمہ واجب ہوتی ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ خالص ودیعت میں بھی اصلاح وابقاء کی چیزیں مالک کے ذمہ ہوتی ہیں۔
سوال راعی جس طریقہ سے حیوان کو چراتا ہے اور اس کی حفاظت بھی کرتا ہے لہذا راعی کی اجرت دونوں پر ہونی چاہیے؟ جواب رائی کا مقصد اصلی چرانا ہے جو اصلاح وابقاء کے باب سے ہے اور حفاظت ضمنی چیز ہے اس لئے راعی کی پوری اجرت را ہن کے ذمہ ہوگی۔ سوال .. اس کی کیا دلیل ہے کہ رائی کا مقصد اصلی حفاظت نہیں ہے؟ جواب اگر کوئی چراگاہ میں سے جانور کو چرالے تو اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جاتا اگر راعی محافظ ہوتا تو مقام محفوظ سے چرانے کی وجہ سے ہاتھ کاٹا جاتا۔
He said: And the hire of the house in which the pledge is preserved is upon the pledgee, and likewise the hire of the guardian and the hire of the shepherd, and the maintenance of the pledge is upon the pledgor. And the fundamental principle is that whatever is needed for the benefit of the pledge and its preservation, it is upon the pledgor, whether there is surplus in the pledge or not, because the property remains in his ownership, and likewise its usufructs are owned by him, so its improvement and preservation are upon him, since it is a cost of his ownership, as in the case of a deposit. And that is like expenditure on its food and drink, and the hire of the shepherd is equivalent to that because it is fodder for the animal. / Translation: And the hire of the house in which the pledge will be preserved is upon the pledgee, and likewise the hire of the guardian and the hire of the shepherd, and the maintenance of the pledge is upon the pledgor. And the general rule is that whatever need arises for the benefit of the pledge and for its preservation, that is upon the pledgor, whether there is excess in the pledge or not, because the property remains in his ownership, and likewise its usufructs are owned by him, so its improvement and preservation are upon him, because it is the expense of his ownership, as in a deposit. And that is like expenditure on its food and drink, and the hire of the shepherd is in the same category because it is fodder for the animal. / Explanation: It has passed previously that the pledge remains in the possession of the pledgee. Now this question arises: upon whom is its maintenance obligatory, and who will pay the rent of the house where it is preserved, and who will pay the wages of the guard employed for its supervision? So regarding this, a general rule has been stated: whatever falls under the category of protection (hifaz), that is upon the pledgee; and whatever falls under the category of improvement and preservation (islah wa ibqa'), that is upon the pledgor. This is the general rule, and in any case this is the ruling, whether the value of the pledge is more than the debt, equal, or less. Therefore, the room in which it is kept is from the category of protection, and likewise appointing a guard is from the category of protection, so the rent of the house and the wages of the guard are obligatory upon the pledgee. And the hire of the shepherd, the maintenance of the pledge, and clothing, all these are upon the pledgor. And the reason is that the property of the pledge is owned by the pledgor, and the usufructs of the pledge are also owned by the pledgor, and whatever is for the improvement and preservation of ownership, that is obligatory upon the owner. This is the reason why in a pure deposit also, the things of improvement and preservation are upon the owner. / Question: The shepherd grazes the animal and also protects it, so the shepherd's wages should be upon both? Answer: The main purpose of the shepherd is grazing, which falls under improvement and preservation, and protection is incidental, so the entire wages of the shepherd are upon the pledgor. / Question: What is the proof that the main purpose of the shepherd is not protection? Answer: If someone steals an animal from the pasture, his hand is not cut off; if the shepherd were a protector, the hand would be cut off for stealing from a protected place.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage provides a key general principle in Hanafi law of mortgage: expenses are divided based on the nature of the action. The principle is derived analogically from the law of deposit (wadi'ah). The pledgee (murtahin) is responsible for costs related to preservation (hifazah), such as rent for storage space and wages of a guard. The pledgor (rahin) is responsible for costs related to maintenance and preservation of the property's value (islah wa ibqa'), such as food for animals, shepherd's wages, and clothing for slaves. The commentary explains the rule with a question-answer: why is the shepherd's entire wage on the pledgor when he also protects? Because the primary purpose is grazing (maintenance), and protection is incidental. This is evidenced by the fact that stealing from a pasture does not trigger the hadd penalty for theft, indicating it is not a protected place. This shows the rigorous use of legal reasoning (istidlal) in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
حفظ (hifz): Preservation or guarding of the pledged property; expenses for this fall on the pledgee.
اصلاح و ابقاء (islah wa ibqa'): Improvement and preservation of the property's value; expenses fall on the owner (pledgor).
راعی (ra'i): Shepherd; included in maintenance costs because the primary purpose is grazing (feeding).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle, whose obligation is the hire of a shepherd for a pledged animal?
A) The pledgee, because he guards the animal.
B) The pledgor, because the shepherd's primary job is grazing the animal, which is maintenance.
C) Shared equally between pledgee and pledgor.
D) The pledgee, because the animal is in his possession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, guarding expenses of a pledge are on the pledgee; maintenance expenses are on the pledgor, based on ownership and analogy to deposit.
